THE GREAT ALL-QUEENSLAND ROBBERY
The black spaces show the lands— 200,000,000 acres —proposed to be handed over gratis to land-grant railway syndicates in Queensland. The white portions represent
the thin slices of the province that will remain—hemmed in from markets on every side by the freeholds of the syndicators—for the people.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required ,<
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year. - - -
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with tvhom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 tvords, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin toil I return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such 'MSS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 8, 1893.
The Public Enemy—M'Ilwraith.
The Queensland general elections take place
in a very few weeks from this date, and
then Thomas' MTlwraith, the mighty
syndicator, will apply to the electors—those
that remain l of them—to express their
approval of his policy. If things go well
with him, it will be the apotheosis of the
corpulent financier of Auchenflower, and his
head will jostle with the heavens—the only
heavens to which MTlwraith and his kind
have ever aspired. It has taken him many
patient years to work out the stupendous
programme which now seems to be on the
verge of accomplishment. The cheap, com-
monplace politician who helped to form'the
tail of the old Macalister Cabinet, the
jobbing Premier who—along with Palmer
and Jonah Perkins —tried to engineer one
small, tentative, apologetic land-grant railway
transaction, and was immediately extin-
guished with ignominy, the loud-mouthed
patriot and bogus Republican who came
back to power to be the Washington df
Australia, the deserted and discredited place-
man who fell from office and went to sulk in
obscurity, the subordinate evil genius of the
Coalition—these are the various phases of
the MTlwraith of the past. And out of
them has been evolved the MTlwraith of
the present, a bloated and apoplectic autocrat
who aspires to run a Hispano - American
dictatorship in Queensland a Guzman
Blanco without his patriotism—a Barrios
without a single one of his noble ideals.
* • #
MTlwraith 's position is unique among
Australian politicians. He has abandoned
his alleged republicanism ; he has practically
dismissed his nominal master, Griffith,
after utterly discrediting that traitor and
wiping his boots upon Griffith’s carcase;
he has bought the Parliament bodily, and
with its aid has disfranchised an enormous
percentage of the democratic electors of
Queensland ; and he would almost seem to
have abandoned even the stereotyped pre-
tence of seeking power for the good of his
country. He has abandoned everythrrTg, in
fact, save the work of wreaking vengeance
upon the common people, and ministering to
the hunger of the ravenous foreign land-
syndicates. “This province for sale, cheap”—
that is the trade-mark of the M‘llwraith
Ministry and its hungry following.
* * * #
There have been many unscrupulous poli-
ticians in Australia’s past history, but none
like M‘llwraith. Neither will there ever
be any like M‘llwraith in the future, for if
he be returned to office there will, in the
near future, be nothing left for his successors
to squander. Compared to him, such fin-
anciers as Vogel sink into insignificance,
and the whole race of reckless wasters, from
the first beginnings of the country down to
the present time, are but as the small dust
in the balance. Nobody, before M‘llwraith’s
time, ever conceived the idea of parting with
all the available public estate of an enormous
province in one act, without getting a solitary
coin into the Treasury in return. The
inspiration of “ selling ” for nothing, in fee
simple, enough land to make an empire, and
professing to confer a benefit upon the
nation thereby, was so vast a treachery as to
be beyond even the most daring of them all.
If MTlwraith remains in office for a very
few years longer he may then die comfort-
ably, with the full assurance that he will
never be forgotten in Queensland. The
curses of a, ruined nation in the throes of
civil war will keep his memory green when
all his contemporaries are forgotten.
* * »
On the preceding page will be found a full
exposition of the programme on which the
Queensland Dictator proposes to appeal to
the nation—or, rather, that section of it
which has not yet been disfranchised by a
cunningly-contrived and cowardly law,
passed by a Parliament of traitors—a Parlia-
ment which, elected as a protest against
black slavery, yet had the effrontery to re-
enact black slavery ; and which, elected also
as a protest against land-grant railways, yet
deliberately dug deep the foundations of the
biggest legislative land-steal in the world's
history. It is a programme well adapted
for pictorial illustration. The parts of the
sketch which are coloured black represent
the public estate which the M‘llwraith-
favoured land-grant railway syndicates are to
receive, without payment, in consideration
for making their own railways across Queens-
land. The railways, when made, are to be
the exclusive'property,of the syndicates, and
to he worked (entirely for their benefit. The
■.speculators who profit by. this wondrous
transaction do not buy their immense mono-
poly from the State —a monopoly which,
with the increase of population, will one day
become an endless gold-mine ; instead, they
receive it gratis, and receive also about half
the province as compensation for receiving it
gratis ! The lying pretence on which this
transaction is based is that it is no longer
possible to borrow money for construction
purposes ; but if only half the proposed
grants were sold in the open market they
would yield enough to build all the proposed
lines, as national property, three times over.
As it is, the country has to pay something
like six times the value of the railways— and
it does not even get the railways after all.
The dimensions of this stupendous fraud
upon posterity can only be gauged by the
map. It sweeps away everything except the
narrow strip along the coast—which the
syndicators and their sympathisers and con-
federates already hold in their individual
capacities as squatters and speculators; a
few attenuated blocks sandwiched in be-
tween the railway-steals—which blocks the
syndicators are even now buying up at 10s.
per acre'; and one death-stricken, ghost-
haunted little patch of barrenness in the far
North, infested by samples of nearly every
malarial disease, insect plague, snake and
assorted reptile on earth—the trackless, sun-
cursed region of Cape York, where even a
syndicate would die of thirst and horror.
This is all that is left for the people of
Queensland and their posterity ; the syndi-
cates are to take the rest. Among all the
men in history who have sold, or attempted
to sell, their country, no one ever planned a
vaster sale-than this. But, after all, The
Bulletin does not charge M‘llwraith with
selling the country. It recognises that ho is
merely giving it away.
# #
We have alluded to the land-grant railway
project as M’llwraiths policy, and in
truth he has no other. It is even open to
doubt if he ever had any other. As far
as can bo gathered from his present atti-
tude he cares nothing for Freetrade,
Protection, Imperialism, Republicanism,
Federation, social problems, or anything
else under the sun. M‘ll wraith’s career
has revealed the hideous weakness of Parlia-
mentary Government—unless accompanied
by the Referendum or some similar means
whereby the people at large can overrule
their parliamentary representatives when
the latter betray their trust—as it was never
before revealed in Australasia, and seldom
in any other land. When MTI wraith’s
friends came into power for the third time
they found themselves virtual masters of a
Legislature which was pledged almost to a
man to abjure Kanaka labour and land-grant
railways. They realised that 90 per cent, of
the politicians around them would gladly go
back'on their oaths if they received sufficient
inducement and could be guaranteed against
the popular vengeance at next election. The
lure was easily found : when all Queensland
was to be eaten up, there was enough induce-
ment among the prospective fragments to
sway twenty such Legislatures. And the
most effectual guarantee against the nation’s
wrath was simply to disfranchise the nation
—or as much of it as seemed likely to vote
against the sale of the country into indus-
trial slavery. And, without the Referendum,
or some such device whereby the nation
can veto the acts of a corrupted Parlia-
ment, there was absolutely no redress, save
by armed insurrection. There has been no
such treachery in Australian history before,
yet, unless the Referendum is introduced, a
similar fraud may be perpetrated any day in
any part of this continent.
# » *
Whether the policy of disfranchisement,
whereby it has been sought to convert
MTlwraith into a Dictator, will or will not
prove absolutely effective, the coming election
must reveal. The Queensland Democracy
has—so far as peaceful means are concerned
—this one chance between it and utter ruin,
and it must make the most of it. If one
single electorate between the border and the
Gulf is left uncontested in the Democratic
interest—if one single Democrat who still
possesses a vote fails to exercise it, the feeble
chance of success is thereby rendered a
shade more feeble still, and the neglected
opportunity is a crime which may never be
repaired. The Democratic leaders must
issue the same proclamation which, in days
of old, used to call out the whole of
the Saxon manhood against a common
enemy: “ Let every man who is not a
nothing leave his home and come.” The
occasion is one when all minor differences
should be sunk --when the unionist and the
non-unionist, the Paraguaylian pilgrim and
those who have scorned the men of Lane’s
crusade as deserters from the cause of Aus-
tralia, the native and the stranger within
his gates, must all unite to save the country
from a brutal rape. If candidates of in-
telligence are scarce it matters little ; what
is wanted is a reliable Parliamentary leader,
one or two able lieutenants, and a following
whose fidelity will be proof against attack.
And the programme of the party should
consist of these points first and above all
things ; The absolute prohibition of land-
grant railways ; the stoppage of the Griffil-
wraith land-sale policy ; the abolition of
Chinese, Kanaka, Indian, and all other forms
of pauper immigration; the establishment of
manhood suffrage pure and simple ; the in-
troduction of the Referendum, as a guarantee
against any repetition of the stupendous
treachery of the Coalition ; and a cast-iron
resolution that no terms shall be made and
no faith kept with MTlwraith —that he
shall be regarded as a common enemy, and
that there shall be no rest nor peace until he
is driven from the Legislature.
* * *
Let no faith be kept with MTlwraith
because he has kept none. His professions
that his huge scheme of railway construction
will provide work and wages for the hungry
population are a mockery ; the work will be
done by the coolies for whose importation
arrangements have already been made in
India, or by some other variety of
cheap imported pauperism. The wages
will be 9d. per diem, and the slop over
of the tide of cheap labour will swamp
every industry in Queensland with the
black labour curse for ages to come. If the
cause of Democracy goes down in the coming
conflict and no other means of redress can
be found, the railway syndicates will own
Queensland —its lands, its huge undeveloped
mineral resources, its banks, its newspaper
press, its means of communication, its
Legislature, its Government—yea, even its
courts of justice. And as for the mass of
the population, its position will be that of the
Bengalee ryot towards his white conqueror—
equally servile and equally hopeless.
• # #
These are the inevitable consequences if
the Democratic party is defeated, and pro-
vided no other means of redress can be
found, and the only other means of redress
is civil war. The logical excuse for civil war
lies, not in the vastness of the interests at
stake, but in the fact that the Coalition, by
its manipulation of the franchise, has de-
liberately deprived a great part of the nation
of its right of self-government, and has
reduced its opponents to political serfdom.
In abolishing a great portion of the con-
stituency it has violated justice as grossly as
did Charles I. when he sought to abolish
Parliament and thereby started on the road
to the scaffold, or Louis Napoleon when he
perpetrated his famous coup d'etat. It has, by
guile and corruption, practically abolished the
principle of Government by the majority,
and under such conditions the right of the
majority to assert itself by any possible
means is beyond dispute. If Premier
MTlwraith will submit his railway and
Kanaka policy to the popular vote under the
same franchise which existed when the last
Parliament was returned, and if his proposi-
tions are endorsed by the nation, then there
is nothing more to be said. In that case, it
will be merely a matter of national suicide,
and the right of a community to commit
suicide is as indefeasible as that of an indi-
vidual. But otherwise he occupies the posi-
tion of a usurper and a tyrant, who must be
dethroned.
Dr. Bevan on the Land Boom.
Onck when Pabkbs was telling a public meeting
of the glory and gain that would accrue to New-
South Wales were he permitted to run” the pro-
vince, a hungry-looking mechanic interrupted.
He wanted to know “straight ” what would be
done to enable engineers to find employment at
decent wages, For once the maker of Austra-
lian history refrained from launching the con
suming lightnings of repartee. He might
according to his Parkesian custom, have told his
questioner to “go out and bark with the other
dogs,” or called him a “ poodle-headed creeping
creature,” or “ a mole-eyed Murray cod.” But
instead, he attacked the problem presented
boldly, and gave a reply which evoked thunders
of approbation from his supporters, and left his
ill-conditioned catechist silent and abashed
He declared his conviction that “the softening
process now at work upon the bases of society
would ultimately tend to greatly ameliorate
the condition of the wage-earning masses.” The
bamboozled audience demanded to know what
the discontented engineer had to say to that
and he narrowly escaped being thrown out, ’
* * *
Dr. Bevan, the fat and sociable professional
Christian of Melbourne (wages, £3O a week)
has lately been in Sydney lecturing upon what
ho saw during his last trip round the world.
It is a good thing for those who cannot take
such trips that somebody else can who doesn’t
mind telling them wdiat it is like. Dr. Bevan
is that sort. He returns from abroad and
chatters pleasantly enough of what he has seen
and done. He is that anomalous thing—an
entertaining clergyman well liked by all
classes. Little pebbles of Church reform and
improved charitable administration are thank-
fully accepted from his hand by those starving
for the bread of justice. The exploiters, too
the men who prey upon their fellows, and, if
they fatten sufficiently thereby, put the penny
of a church window or a contribution to the
stipend in the slot of salvation, like Dr. Bevan.
He sometimes admonishes them in an abstract
way, and preaches against the Mammon of un-
righteousness. He digs them in the ribs and
says, “ You are sad dogs who will not inherit
the Kingdom of Heaven,” as Lady Tiffins
used to smite a “ wicked man ” with her fan.
And, as he was in Sydney, the local Daily
Telegraph took the opportunity of ascertain-
ing his views on several subjects, particularly
the land-boom and its results. Some time ago
Dr. Bevan, in a general way, thanked Gon for
the chastening influence of boom, and what he
has to say now is on the same lines. For
vacuous inconsequentiality, Parkes could not
beat this:—
The depression iias been of very great advantage to us.
It will stay the great public expenditure, it will call men
in the middle classes back to a simpler way of living, and
it will break down that irritated feeling between capital
and labour which has given rise to so many difficulties in
the past. Workmen and employers alike are sufferers in
a common disaster, and this time a great misfortune will,
I think, tend gradually to bring them more closely
together.
# # *
According to this, what is required to secure
peace on earth and good will among men is
enough land-booms. God works in a mysterious
way, and Dr. Bevan recognises the land-syndi-
cate as a means whereby He anneals together
the worker who wants fair wages and the em-
ployer who never gives more than he can help,
wisely regulates Governmental expenditure,
and advances righteousness. All that is neces-
sary is to corner city and suburban lands and
gamble with them until the people are stone-
broken into inability to buy groceries and
driven to beg from the sixpenny restaurants
such shreds as the swine do eat. Make the
great majority of the people of a province
poorer, more dependent on such employment as
can be had ; hand them over, under the whip of
a necessity sharper than a serpent’s tooth, to em-
ploying drivers, themselves made more anxious
about the securing of profits ; render it neces-
sary to take more in taxation than ever before
from their earnings, and Dr, Bevan thinks you
are on the right track to a rational solution of
social and governmental difficulties. Anyhow,
he optimistically opines that everything will
ultimately turn up trumps, and goes on twitter-
ing to the money-changers of what he saw in
America—“almost universally a mechanical
hardening of nature—a nation of worshippers of
the almighty dollar,” to wit,
« • •
The Melbourne land boom was not a “great
misfortune. ” It w-as a colossal villainy. There
was nothing in the nature of accidental or
unforeseeable calamity about it. The Queens-
land flood could be called a misfortune,
because it was outside the power of
men to know of its coming and (buildings
once having been erected on the low-lying
lands) prevent it. The Melbourne land boom
was a conspiracy against the community
entered into by a few, chiefly pillars of the
churches, who, in the interests of stock-jobbing,
hoed a row in one or other of the gardens of
grace. Certain lands known to be necessary
to industrial uses were secured and held against
use. The men who did this thing were enemies
of the public, as truly as if, holding a supply of
corn, they had burned the harvests of the
country to secure famine prices for it. If a
Brisbane merchant, having a stock of goods
stored on high ground, had been able to cause
the Queensland floods, and had done so with the
object of making a fortune out of consequent
public need, he would have been the moral peer
of a Melbourne land boomer. The first care of
the syndicates was to find out in what direction
settlement was most likely, under natural
circumstances, to extend. Their next was
to buy the land there so that it
could not extend until they had been hand-
somely squared. The buying was often done
on paper, payment being contingent upon
ultimate sales, so that the chief gamblers used
loaded dice. In the expressive phraseology of
the share-market, they “stood on velvet.” They
could not lose much, while their gain would be
in proportion to, and made up from, the loss of
the people.
# * #
When a network of land “ corners ” environed
the city, the price of land naturally x-ose. That
is to say, the amount the cornerers could take
from the public earnings increased. Fictitious
sales were quoted as genuine, and everything
conceivable done to attract buyers. They were
attracted in due course, and, the brief reign of
unreal land-values having set in, were enabled
to sell again to others at a profit. This
emboldened them to try again and again, and
to the comparatively thin ranks of the pro-
fessional land-sharks were soon added a swarm
of ambitious amateurs. Rapid fortunes were


■ealised by men who couldn’t earn salt at any
kind of useful work, and after awhile half the
people in Melbourne took to smoking nine-
rienny cigars and trying to unload residential
sites upon each other. The papers became litho-
graphic paeans of town allotments, interspersed
vvitli letterpress accounts of sales effected
a tul leading* articles on the great increase of
national wealth. There was no such increase,
but a diminution greater than flood or drought
could cause. Wealth consists of things needful
for use and produced by labour. All that had
happened was that a few thousand persons had
been started bidding against each other for the
privilege of blackmailing production and pre-
venting the creation of wealth. Capitalised values
became far higher than any return from land
could yield ; rents rose until shopkeepers were
starved out, and wages fell correspondingly.
# * #
Some of the chief conspirators came to grief
by over-boldness. But the wiliest of them
prospered exceedingly, and unloaded in time to
escape the consequences of collapse. Providence
watched over those of them who watched the
progress of the land-mania closely, and calcu-
lated exactly how far it would go. And it will
do the same thing every time. While the chief
and indispensable element of national produc-
tion can be secured by a few and held for a
rise there will be land booms, and one will
profit by the loss of many. If men are allowed
to make money by preventing other men from
earning a living, they will have a try for it, and
some will succeed. The desolating scourge
of land-booms could be easily and effectively
dealt with. If the owner of a thousand-pound
vacant allotment were taxed even as heavily as
the owner of a thousand-pound house, he would
i. ;t hold it very long lor a rise, and the rise
generally wouldn’t come off if he did. But this
remedy is too drastic for Dr. Beyan. Not being
among those who have to live by applying their
labour to land, or who must buy or lease a site
for any business purpose, he thinks things are
going on nicely, and will personally await the
softening processes of time. He recognises the
evil of land monopoly, but that is, of course, in
the abstract, and he would handle it deli-
cately. The heavy interest the belated syndi-
cates are now compelled to pay on their
investments he regards as a sufficiently heavy
tax. In order to temper the wind to the shorn
lambs of the syndicate, he would have their
lands locked up from use for the present. With
that benevolent object he would allow the
millions of pounds’ worth of value which the
people of Victoria have conferred upon the
lands of Melbourne to continue flowing unin-
terruptedly into a few private bank accounts,
while the province starves for revenue and its
workers are taxed up to the eyes.
The Political Economy of Daily
Journalism.
In a leaderette, of March 27, the S.M. Herald,
dealing with “ the disastrous failure of the
cotton-strike in Lancashire,” argues that “ those
who assume the guardianship of the rights of
working-men ” must, sooner or later, recognise
“ that the rate of wages must be governed by
the price the article will bring.” Further, that
the extension and improvements of machinery
“ inevitably necessitate a diminution in the
cost of production which means diminished
wages to the workmen.” The Bulletin does
not remember to have met, on any previous
occasion, more examples of fallacious reasoning
in so brief a space.
# # #
In the first place, it never was, and never will
be, true that the price of an article determined
the rate of wage, but it always was and
always will, while present conditions en-
dure, be true that the rate of wages largely
determines the price of articles. As for
the statement that a cheapening in the
cost of production must necessarily be
followed by a reduction in the exchange-value
of goods, that is not essential if usual; but it is
much less true that a lessening in the cost of
articles must necessarily be followed by a
reduction of wages. An all-round decrease in
the price of goods would doubtless be followed,
and justly, by a lowering of wages, but the
reduction would be more apparent than real,
being balanced by an increase in the purchasing
power of money. Looking at the Herald’s
argument ethically and economically, it will be
seen to be alike unjust, unwise, and immoral
that an addition to a community’s power of
producing wealth should result in a lessening of
the amount diverted to the personal uses of its
producers. Suchacourse of conduct would divert
invention from its avowed purpose. Everyone
speaks of labour-saving, no one of waye-lessening
machinery. Therefore, it must be immoral to
turn community-developed gifts into engines
which lower certain men’s standard of living,
and thereby increase their tendencies to be
ignorant and evil-disposed. Thus to act is
simply to increase the burdens and responsi-
bilities of the State, to fill asylums with the
prematurely aged and ill-developed, to fill gaols
with economically-manufactured criminals.
# # #
Neither in the ethics of Christianity or any
other faith will the Herald find an apologist.
On the face of things, it is unjust that an
increase of the national capacity for wealth-
making should result in diminishing the wealth
receipts of a certain class. Moreover, the pro-
ceeding is as absurd as it is unjust, and even more
unwise than either. Consider; The community’s
capacity for wealth-production is increased,
and, therefore, the sum of wealth produced.
Hurely the obvious course for the sane and not
too selfish would be to give opportunities for an
increase of consumption co-equal with the
aggregation of wealth ? No, says the Herald,
the moral, just, and wise method is to follow an
increase of production by such a decrease of
wages that a decrease of consumption must
result! The Herald does not recommend a
decrease of profits ; oh, no. But an increase of
profits, rent, and interest, even up to the mar-
gin of increased production, would not cor-
respondingly increase consumption.
*' # •
The wealthy classes have already readied
their limit of consumption. A further increase
of their wealth means to them, not an advanced
purchase of the ordinary commodities cheapened
by invention, but an augmentation of invest-
ment (meaning a further increase in production)
and a slight swelling of expenditure in the direc-
tion of luxuries. But the world’s market-men
cater for the multitude, and the multitude is
made up of wage-earners whose earnings go, to
the last penny, in weekly purchases of com-
modities. It necessarily follows, then, that any
considerable multiplication of wealth which is.
not followed by a corresponding augmentation
of the general power to purchase (the Herald
asserts, on the contrary, that a lessening of the
power to purchase should follow) must defeat
its own object and result in the ruin of all con-
cerned. The only exception to this rule is to
be found in the case of serf-peoples who do not
produce for the use of their own community,
but for those of other countries. Any such
departure among free peoples from the economic
law that consumption should keep pace with
production must result in what is idiotically
known as over-production—for which “under
consumption ” would be a more truly descriptive
term—depression, pauperism, commercial crisis
and national bankruptcy. Working-men are
happily saved from such a “guardianship of their
rights ” as that laid down by the S.M. Herald.
It ought to understand the position as The
Bulletin has stated it. If it does and yet
promulgates different opinions, then its iniqui-
ties are manifold, but we prefer to look at its
economic heresies merely as an example of the
blind leading the blind, and not as a deliberate
attempt to mislead. It is usually the Herald’s
misfortune more than its fault that it is an un-
conscious disseminator of everything that makes
for irreligion, revolution, anarchy, injustice,
criminality, discontent, and Mammon-worship.
Doubtless, the day will come when a journal
publishing such disturbing and upheaving
articles as the one quoted from will render
itself liable to have its plant confiscated and its
circulation summarily stopped. But it is not
yet.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Payment of Members.
Ox no question of social politics are civilised
countries better agreed than on the justice of
paying members of Parliament for their ser-
vices. The cabled announcement that the
Gladstoxe Government has accepted the pro-
posal of the Radicals and will introduce a Pay-
ment of Members Bill in the Commons brings
England almost abreast with the great body of
civilised States. England is the only country
governed by western ideas which does not pay its
members of Parliament directly or indirectly.
In Italy and Spain members are not directly
paid but are rewarded for their services by free
travelling passes and various other tangible
favours. For the rest, all countries in the
northern hemisphere pay their members. But
while there is this practical unanimity as to the
principle there is the utmost difference as
to the amounts paid. This promises to
be the main point of contention in Australia
also. In the United States members draw
£lOOO a year; in France, the next great Re-
public, £1 per day ; and in Republican Switzer-
land ten shillings in the Council and six
shillings in the Lower House. In Denmark
they receive fifteen shillings, and in Portugal a
guinea. In Sweden the session is limited to
four months, for which each member receives
£66; in Norway members receive fourteen
shillings per day for as many days as the
session lasts. Germany gives ten shillings per
day, Austria £l, and Greece £2O per month to
senators, and £lO to deputies. A want of uni-
formity is to be expected owing to the different
value of money in different countries, but the
root of the difference lies in the different values
attached to legislative work by different com-
munities. Money is of about the same value all
over Australia, yet there is a near prospect of
each Legislature having a tariff different to all
the others. One more interesting feature in the
payment of members question is the manner
in which communities having two Houses value
the services of both classes of representatives.
The United States and France pay both alike ;
Switzerland makes the difference already noted;
but with regard to Switzerland it should be
remembered that the National Council is some-
thing more than what we know here as an
Upper House. Portugal pays its Peers and
Commons alike, as do also Germany and Austria,
while Greece pays twice as much to its senators
as to its deputies. The minor proposals over
which Australian legislators so often wax
eloquent are substantive laws in several coun-
tries. In many, payment per day is limited to
the number of days a session actually lasts, and
in some, as in Sweden, members are fined for
every day they are absent without sufficient
cause. The inference from all this is that a
man can pronounce in favour of payment of
members with the moral certainty that the
civilised thought of the world is at his back,
but that upon the other matters there is room
for experiment.
In New South Wales and Queensland Parlia-
ments the lawyers are, as a class, the most per-
sistent opponents of payment of members. Is
this an instance in political evolution of what
Darwin, in physiological evolution, calls
“throwing back?” In Edward lll.’s time,
when payment of members was the law in the
English Parliament, the people were so over-
ridden by lawyers that a decree was passed
ordaining that if lawyers were elected they
should not be paid. Soon after, the lawyers
started a crusade against payment. In the
wholly different conditions of Australia, where
every man is expected to work and to require
payment for his work, is the antipathy to pay-
ment of members manifested by local lawyers
explainable as are the crooked eyes of a child
of straight-eyed parents but of crooked-eyed
ancestors ?
Another case of alleged assault upon a young
Melbourne girl has been summarily dismissed
by a crowded (amateur) Bench which had rolled
up to see justice done. In this last instance,
the accused person was held guiltless on the
ground that the girl, being an admitted liar on
some points, was an inevitable liar in all direc-
tions. For a proved lie a motive could be seen
at a glance; for the supposed swearing there
was no very apparent reason brought forward.
A violated wench was asked to “ put a n one ”
to her violator, and she put one. Moreover,
she threw in certain particulars relating to the
assault which were so flatly contradicted by the
young man’s mother that the dismissal of the
case, without any mention of an indictment for
perjury one way or the other, was in itself a
caricature of justice. It seems that parsons
and parsons’ sons, in Melbourne, have no occa-
sion to clear their characters of suspicion in the
ordinary way.. A sympathetic Bench calls the
girl a perjuress, and there the matter ends.
Sometimes the crowd outside sets up a cheer,
and sometimes it howls exceedingly, but in
either case every thing is as right as right can be.
Frazer, in his anecdotic “ Disraeli,” thus
describes the genesis of the C.M.G. order :
When the lonian Islands were given up, the Order of
Sts. Michael and George fell into desuetude ; it had been
instituted at Lord Guildford’s suggestion on finding
that the members of the lonian Parliament actually
walked to St. Petersburg with a view to obtaining a
decoration. It was necessary to counteract this ; and St.
George for England, and St. Michael as the leader of the
celestial hierarchy, neither of whom were in any way
connected with the lonian Islands, were made the patrons
of the new order. The motto—“ Auspicium melioris
sevi” was and is singularly inappropriate; it should be
changed to “ Imperi Porrecta Majestas!” Of course,
when the Islands were given up there was no longer a
purpose in the Order. It occurred to me that it might be
made most useful as a reward for colonial services. I
brought forward the question in the House of Commons.
I had studied Lord Palmerston carefully, and I felt quite
certain that he would reply by telling the story, already
an ancient one, that William iv., at Brighton, complain-
ing of the persecution by the Mayor of some large town,
who pursued him even to his marine retreat, the King’s
son, Lord Adolphus Fitzclarence said, “I should ‘ Guelph’
him, Sire, at once,” another version being that the
King said, “I shall have to Guelph him,” and Lord A.
replied, “And serve him right, sire !”—that is, give him
the Guelphic Order. So accurately had I taken Lord
Palmerston’s measure that, notwithstanding I had nar-
rated this story, he said in his reply, “To repeat the
story already told by the honourable Baronet’’—and
went through it again. I remember Sir James Douglas,
at that time one of the few remaining K.C.M.G.’s, giving
tongue loudly during my harangue ; he did not want to
be diluted. However, the next year the order was re-
established on the lines suggested by myself, and I feel
sure that the broad blue and red ribbon has made many
an honest heart happy, and, to use the ivords of Falcon-
bridge, many a “ Joan a lady /”
To Australians this story carries its own moral.

Duuker, who swore the information against
the Mercantile Bank people, admits that he
would much rather there had been no pro-
ceedings ; but he “ had to do his duty,” Like-
wise, he admits that the Press which “ wrote
what was honest ” practically forced the pro-
ceedings. It is in the extraction of this sort of
evidence by cross-examination that David
Gaunson seems to find a mysterious comfort.
Gaunson’s one idea is to get indirectly an
opinion that bank prosecutions are calculated
to shake public confidence, whereupon he yells,
to all intents and purposes, “ Why don’t you
stop it?” Every now and then the Crown
counsel remind GaunsoN that his brazen-faced
cross-examining “is doing all the harm ” ; but
the hint is thrown away. Since the hot-headed,
ready-tongued attorney lost his seat in Parlia-
ment, and ceased to play at being a Radical, he
has become the champion of silence as regards
public scandals. Where once he would have
made an object of himself in demanding an
inquiry, he now argues that true patriotism
consists in shutting up. He comes into court
bearing a banner with the strange device,
“ Mum !”
Thus a Bulletix correspondent :
Re the coming theft of our railways, shall I tell you
what it will mean to me and to thousands of others in a
similar position? After the railway had been completed
to a certain town, I took up 160 acres of land and spent
on it several hundreds of pounds—all the money I
possessed and all I was able to earn for many years—in
improvements and cultivation. The fact that the State
owned the means of communication was regarded by me
as a guarantee that it would never be used to squeeze me,
as the American farmer is being squeezed by the railway
kings there. Without the railway my land is absolutely
valueless. It is only by reason of the moderate freights
charged that lam able to make a living. Now, suppose
the railway king got possession of the line, he could
either raise my freight or reduce that of my competitors
until I found'myself, by a little manoeuvring on his part,
indebted to him for the carriage of the season’s crop in an
amountfar exceeding what the sale of the produce realised.
He could then easily take my land and the improvements
upon it, and kick me out. He could do hs same all along
the line, and after getting possession of all the farms de-
pending upon it, he could lower the freights and let them
to tenants, then gradually sell out the farms to them, and
as soon as the money was all paid over and additional im-
provements made, he . ould raise the freights again and
absorb No. 2. He could send missionaries to each of the
various European countries in turn and induce immi-
grants to settle on his line, getting rid of them as soon as
desirable and re-settling the line with a new batch. A
privately-owned line is simply a man-trap. Boiled down
it simply amounts to—“ Will you walk into my parlour,”
&c.—W.H.M.

There is a bold, broad humour in the
stated determination of the Vic. Government to
suppress dummy transactions in bank shares, now
that the once roaring “bull” has drawn in his
horns and become a “ bear.”
THE TWO SIDES OF THE DUMMY.
It was not meet to legislate
Against the dummy buyer ;
He laboured bravely to inflate
And send the prices higher;
He purchased shares the livelong day,
Which kept the market “hummy,”
And everybody used to pray
“O Lord, preserve the dummy !” .
When Matthew Davies scattered gold
And’consciences wei'e aisy ;
In those ecstatic days of old
The dummy was a “ daisy. ’'
His dealings did not vex the Crown,
And, though his game wasflummy,
No Carter moved tor putting down'
That omnipresent dummy.
But when the fatness turned to lean,
And pastures green grew yeller,
The dummy changed his mood and mien—
He started as a seller.
From low to lower dropped the shares,
And everyone looked glummy ;
A change came o’er financial prayers—
“O Lord, confound the dummy !”
An evil was the dummy then
Who once had been a blessing ;
“This conduct,” said the “ leading men,”
“ Needs very prompt suppressing.”
In bearing down and raising price
\Ve, therefore, see (how rummy!)
-The difference ’twixt simple vice
And virtue in the dummy.

In London alone there are said to be
300,000 domestic servants. There are, however,
only 50,000 policemen.
Speaker Tom Bent has the “cheek” to
Suppose, or to assert, that he has been a marked
man ever since the time when, being Minister of
Vic. Railways, he “ took upon himself to abolish
to a certain extent the free passes to the Press.”
Thomas doesn’t regard himself as the typical
blusterer who hangs like a millstone round the
neck of his bleeding country—as a red-faced
incubus to the development of political civilisa-
tion, created by the ghastly ignorance and mean
local interests of his Brighton constituents. No
human power can ever convince this painful speci-
men of a Speaker that his old-time victory over
George Higinbotham at the poll was a national
calamity. The gentleman positively believes that
newspaper reporters will snort at him from genera-
tion to generation because he took away free
passes from the Press, whilst offering special baits
for people to pitch their tents at Brighton. Bent’s
bounce is insufferable. Of all his doings in that
dim railway long ago, nothing is remembered now
save that he once rushed from his office in his
shirt-sleeves shrieking for somebody’s gore. Bent
present is quite sufficient for the average jour-
nalist to object to, without troubling about Bent
past.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “Man to Man.”
Lyceum “The Bohemian Girl.”

Alexandra Drama.
“ Miss Esmeralda,” the most operatic of
the Gaiety repertoire, was revived at Sydney
Lyceum, April 1, with a few changes in the cast,
and a great rush of paint to the chorus. Addie
Conyers (still in skirts) played the “dear Mes-
raeriser ” in the careless Gaiety way that seems so
hard for other people to acquire. Though the
music was both above and below her there were
encores and market-baskets for her principal solo.
Lelia Roze, as Phoebus, seemed too occupied in
remembering to keep her heels together, and that
she musn’t be unladylike, to swamp Nellie Barren
in the part —also, she suffered greatly from having
nobody to wink at in the side boxes. The Gaiety
“regulars” in the old harness played with their
usual measure of success as a general thing.
Alice Leamar seemed longing for fresh galleries
to conquer, and a mist seemed to have passed
over Courtneidge’s glory as Corporal Gringoire.
Lonnen has a lot to do as Claude Frollo, but he
bounded through the last production of the
Gaiety season most springfuliy. Haslem put
melodrama into his sketch of Quasimodo, and the
floating Alice Lethbridge fluttered like a saltatory
Jack-o’-Lantern, luring to artistic destruction the
beefy society girl who attempts to follow her fairy
footsteps. The new drop, by Goatcher, was
heartily applauded. It represents a trio of young
ladies, in economical clothing, casually gossiping
in mid-air and surrounded by artistically-painted
white satin curtains, which threaten to fall and
hide them. It is a most artistic and taking thing.
»* * *
English Opera steps into the Gaiety Co.’s
shoes at Sydney Lyceum this Saturday night, when
the season opens with Balfe’s “ Bohemian Girl ”
—perhaps the most barrel-organed opera in the
world. Annis Montague appears as Arline, Charles
Turner as Thadeus, assisted by Edward Farley,
Cicely Staunton, Leopold, Rosevear and others,
“Carmen,” good old “Maritana,” and the less
known “Lily of Killarney,” follow the “Girl,”
which is billed for six nights.
** * *
The return of Alfred Dampier to Sydney
was hailed by an ovation whicli filled the Royal
to the doors and hung its tail out on the stairs
to cool. N For several years past there has been a
certain indefinable craving in the public mind of
that city—a longing for something that was
neither drink nor tobacco-juice, but which refused
to be more definitely located—the sentiment of the
infant howling in the night and knowing not the
wherefore of his howl. But now the people realise
that what they wanted was Dampier. That
gentleman is one of the institutions of New South
Wales, and his absence creates a void that no one
else can satisfy. Not even the most gorgeous
of actor managers with ten times his re-
sources in the way of limelight and blue
paint could fill Dampier’s place; and therefore
Sydney rose with one unanimous vociferation to
welcome him back. He looks no older than he did
when he hung out at the forgotten Gaiety years
ago, neither, for that matter, does daughter Lily,
whose pathetic dark eyes are as bright as ever.
Only it is gratifying to note that this young lady
isn’t so doleful in her methods as she used to be ;
in former times her one serious defect was that
she played as if a secret sorrow lay heavy on
her chest, but this feature isn’t nearly so notice-
able as it used to bo. “ Robbery Under Arms ” is
just the same as of old; a great spectacle
in ten exceedingly beautiful scones over
which Mr. Tischbnuer lias spent many
pounds of paint and a lot of valuable genius.
There are enougii precipices, mountain Ranges,
patches of summer sea, bits of dark-green bush,
forest views, sunsets and thunderstorms among
the ten scenes to make a highly respectable pic-
ture-gallery. The company numbers a hun-
dred or thereabouts, among them being Messrs.
Hodson, Leston, and Piimmer, Miss Julia Mer-
ton, and other reliable favourites. Miss Rose
Dampier also earns a reasonable amount of
applause, and though the public hasn’t quite
made up its mind about this young lady
the general impression is favourable. But
the beloved Alfred, as the gentlemanly bush-
ranger, and Miss Lily performing reckless
feats on a brown horse, are the mainstays of the
show. “Robbery Unde'r Arms” has the great
merit of being distinctly Australian and is of the
bush bushy ; the mere fact that there are in it a
person named Jim, and four characters who are
more or less called Bill, puts this beyond dis-
pute. The four include Arizona Bill, Whistling
Bill, King Bill, and Bill, the small boy, and these
alone are enough to command success,

“A Village Priest” will be put up at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday. The “ Priest ”is
almost unadulterated tragedy from the first line
to the last j still,, in Melbourne, which is
not usually a tragedy-loving city in these
days, it proved one of the best among the
B. &B. Oo.’s many successes. No change is yet
announced at Her Majesty’s, where the public is
still trying hard to think out the full and complete
mystery of the railway collision, and, Up to date,
time has written no wrinkle on the azure brow of
this tremendous smash, Mr. H. Barrington,
formerly of the Alhambra, is now lessee and
manager at the Opera House, where new
comedians, corner-men, ventriloquists, banjoists,
and dancers have lately been laid on with a lavish
hand.
** * *
A great outbreak of high-kickers, about
twenty-five ladies dressed in electric lights, the
Spanish Students, and a frantic song and dance,
described as the “Rowdie-Dowdie Boys,” are
recent attractions at Sydney Tivoli. A Iso, Harry
Rickards now touches a responsive chord in the
great soul of the audience with a new London
song, “ My Old Dutch,” which, on more than one
occasion, has led to him being recalled seven times
in succession. The secret of the immense excite-
ment over “My Old Dutch” is not distinctly
apparent, but no one can argue against a success—
especially one which shakes the building from its
chimney-top to its lowest foundations.
** * *
The Jubilee Singers, a new lot and minus
poor Johnson, raised the mournful negro songs once
more at Sydney Centenary Hall on Monday
night. And the city which goes mad over the
coster howl and the person who has chucked up
the push for his donah came in large numbers,
and with much enthusiasm, to hear them.
Such camp meeting hymns as “Judge-
ment Day is Rolling Round,” “ Roll, Jordan,
Roll,” and “Did the Lord deliver Daniel,”
apparently made the greatest impression. Reli-
gious sentiment evidently goes best when it is
banged out to tunes that are fast and furious,
while the venerable, slow-going hymn falls on
rocky ground. Mr. Orpheus M'Adoo, who runs
the show, and Misses Ball and Mattie Allen are
among the strongest features in the new combina-
tion, but however good they may be, The Bulle-
tin would still like to. see the lost Johnson
rescued from his troubles, and put up again, clothed
and in his right mind, to sing “A Hundred
Fathoms Deep,” or thereabouts. Nothing can
quite obliterate Johnson’s memory, somehow.
** * *
The story of "The Mountebanks” is gaudily Sicilian,
It doesn’t introduce you to a commonplace civilian :
The chorus-men are bandits, while the ladies pass for
peasantry,
Whose only occupation is a daily round of pleasantry.
There's a dapper tenor yokel most extravagantly charming
(His patent-leather shoea are scarcely suitable to farming),
An unfortunate contralto treats this darling as a fetish,
But he’s gone upon “another” who is. callously coquet-
tish.
The rival ladies quarrel, as the custom of their sex is,
Till a wizard kicks the bucket, leaving naught to pay his
exes.,
Except a little bottle full of magic liquor, dooming
Its drinkers to conversion to the parts they were assuming.
Then people who were rummy turn unquestionably
rummer:
The woe is Flora Graupner and her Miserable Mummer !
They had played at being figures in a travelling museum,
So their innards turn to clockworks ; and the bandits sing
Te Deum,
Having dressed in monkish garments with a view to deeds
unlawful
(The girls they meant to marry think that celibacy’s
awful.)
A tender, blooming bride becomes a gouty dame on
crutches,
The yokel is a duke, and the contralto is a duchess
And the maiden he was “gone on” finds the love she
simulated
Is an unrequited passion for the man she under-rated,
There has never been such trouble since the time of Cain
and Abel,
But luckily the bottle of the wizard had a label;
And when that bit of paper at a candle flame is lighted
The awkward situation is immediately righted,
For everyone recovers with an incidental shiver,
And Flora Graupner’s “ works ” are superseded by her
liver.
• # *.
“ The Mountebanks,” produced at Mel-
bourne Princess’s on Saturday, is a comic opera of
the earlier Gilbertian kind. The characters fall
victims to a philtre, as in “The Sorcerer,” and
the effect produced upon them is exactly opposite
to the influence of “The Palace of Truth,” where
all the inmates were compelled to come out as
their real selves. Here we have them
changed to what they were feigning to be when
they drank the wizard’s mixture,'the humour of
which idea is most eccentric in the case of George
Lanri and Flora Graupner, whose humanity
becomes merged into automatic figures of, Hamlet
and Ophelia.. At first hearing “The Mounte-
banks ” seemed many degrees less taking than it
might have been. The best points of the dialogue
were but feebly interpreted in most cases, and
neither the Conductor nor the principals made the
music “go ” as it probably will go presently.
Under any circumstances the hurried finale must
be a disappointment. It was cut short, one
imagines, that the opera might be completed
before Alfred Cellier died. A chronic fogginess in
the voice of May Pollard,, the prima donna of this
occasion, prevents her from making the most of
the coquette character, but she looks and acts to
good advantage. On Saturday, too, May im-
proved as she went along and brought forth some
capital high notes. Braoy was brighter and more
chirpy than of late, but Leumane, who once pro-
mised to come out as a funny man, has gone in
again. Howard Vernon and Ida Osborne were
too rough and too tame respectively, while the
automatic business aforementioned, though comic
enough at the first attempt, will beat a lot more
Working up. Ryley, as a bandit transformed into
a monk, will also stand improvement. In fact*
every distinct feature of “The Mountebanks’”
initial performance might have been a little better;
but the general get-up of the show, for which
Gordon has painted two beautiful scenes, indi-
cated managerial expectation of a long and pro-
sperous run.
&* * *
George Darrell duly carried out his threat
of reopening Melbourne Royal with an entirely
new T and original racing drama, and at 10 p.m. his
docile steed, Caloola, may be seen wobbling in for
the Melbourne Cup with several spavined rivals
wobbling after. The straggle for the Cup is a
sick spectacle as compared with a circus steeple-
chase, therefore, “ The Double Event ” cannot be
honestly described as the author’s masterpiece.
Even his liberality in the matter of horses is un-
fortunate, seeing that they all fly across the stage
at such a ghastly jog-trot, and simply aggravate
an absurd effect. Now, if George had written an
epidemic which would carry off the other horses
after the numbers went up, and leave Caloola to
walk over for the Cup, then the victory of the
gallant animal might have been taken seriously.
Or if George had entered himself for the
Oup, and came romping in by six lengths,
with his noble head in his chest, and
a band playing ! Our oath ! George fully ex-
tended, showing his heels to the quadrupeds
from just below the distance-post! “ The Double
Event ” is a drama that consists on the one hand
of George Darrell backing Caloola, and on the
other of a seedy villain who doesn’t mean to let it
win. When George wants to book a few more
thousands about his famous colt he goes to the
villain, and the villain lays the odds, The man
isn’t good enough to trust, for five shillings, but
George has a beautiful faith in the infallibility of
credit betting. If the “Tiger” were to offer two
millions to one against Caloola, the confiding
George would take him at his word and sneer
haughtily. He bills himself as “a plunger,” and
bases his human interest upon the inanity of his
wagers. His noblest characteristic is an airy
determination to be hopelessly ruined if Caloola
can’t win the “double,” and when he sits gazing
in the moonlight at the stable door, with a book-
maker’s fair daughter by his side, he talks to her in
the language of touts, like Claude Melnotte quot-
ing the latest training reports. It is most afflict-
ing to hear him tell the darling of bis heart that
Oaloola’s gallop gave great satisfaction, and to
hear him cry, in the hour of the villain’s apparent
triumph, that he (George) will, if necessary, allow
his horse to complete the Double Event on three
legs. As an instance of self-sacrifice this beats
George’s record up to date. Robert Inman, Kelly,
Charles Brown, George Carey, Poulton, Greena-
way, Edith Blande, Ida Herbert, and Jennie Lee
play parts in this original racing drama, and
George Darrell plays the rest in a decidedly im-
proved style. He gives an appearance of sanity
to his character, which is no small thing to do
under the circumstances. But his style is ham-
pered, so to speak, by the lack of a red-flannel
shirt and a few yards.of strong dialogue.

The Alexandra made another melancholy
appeal to the public on Saturday, but the new
season started with the death-mark on its clammy
brow, An Irish drama, entitled “ Ayma-na-
Nick,” was placed before a meagre audience in a
meagre manner, and after the curtain fell the
management treated the company and sundry
other people to a banquet on the stage. A pity
that this lavish outlay wasn’t expended in sand-
wiches and beer for the people who bought tickets.
They looked hungry enough as they left the
theatre. People visit a theatre to be amused —•
not to be preached at.

Melbourne Opera House variety show was
furbished up again on Saturday, and many other
places were in holiday request. The Seraphina
Orchestra (a sera pinna being a glorified concertina)
obtained a respectful hearing at the Town Hall
again on Monday, and the “ Jerusalem ” Cyclo-
rama has more charms for true Christianity than
could be found in “ Waterloo.” Also the Exhi-
bition entertainment ranges from dumb panto-
mime to cow-milking by machinery. Altogether,
Melbourne seems to be having quite a burst.
BEHIND THE SCENES.
The actor struts his little hour.
Between the limelight and the band j
The public feel the actor’s power,
Yet nothing do they understand
Of all the touches here and there
That make or mar the actor’s part.
They never see, beneath the glare,
The artist striving after art.
To them it seems a labour slight
Where nought of study intervenes ;
Yon see it in another light
When once you’ve been behind the scenes.
For though the actor at his best
Is, like a poet, born not made,
He still must study with a zest
And practise hard to learn his trade.
So, whether on the actor’s form
The stately robes of Hamlet sit,
Or as Macbeth he rave and storm,
Or plays burlesque to please the pit;
’Tis each and all a work of art,
That constant care and practice means—
The actor Who creates a part
Has done his work behind the scenes.

“Miss Genevieve Ward gives the utmost impressiveness
to the part of the malignant Eleanor, even the harsh*
unpleasant voice being an advantage in this instance,”
says a London paper. The assumption that Genevieve’s
harshness of voice is chronic makes one smile. “ Porget-
me-Not,” as we remember her in Australia, could talk
like a lute, or the lost chord of a cathedral organ. She
had a be-eautiful voice.
When Thornton was running “The Private Secretary”
and “Sweet Lavender,” At Melb. Alexandra, he used to
make a point of counting, from the stage, the number of
people in the dress-circle. Then he would require full
particulars respecting the dead-heads, and before the
show was through he would know exactly who had paid
and who hadn’t. Thus, Thornton was able to say how
much of the circle applause represented hard cash, A
very careful man was the llev. Spalding.

supplies most of the brains"-though, indeed, the father of
fair Ellen’s “morganatic” children was a gifted man.

Wal. Harland, managing Woddyear’s Circus for widow
Woodyear, got his hippodrome wrecked by an infuriated
riiob of Chinese at the coolie-shipping port of Swatow. A
coolie had got some blood on his face in a crowded
squeeze, and the Chinkies got the idea that Harland had
wounded the man with a sword. Everything portable
was broken up and only the horses were saved. Harland
got no redress from the British Consul —“a veritable old
woman, timorous, shaky, utterly unfit for responsibility”
—and was glad to clear out to avoid the fulfilment of a
mob threat to kill the horses.

When G. C. Miln was touring the back-blocks of U.S.A.,
he one night observed an old lady in the front row dis-
solved in tears. Flattered and gratified, he sent an usher
to say that the tragedian would like to see her after the
show. When they met, Miln remarked, in those richly-
buttered tones, “My acting moved you, I perceived.”
“ It did that, sir,” said the old dame ; “you see, sir, I’ve
got a son myself play-actin’ down in Kentucky some-
where, an’ it broke me all up to think that mebbe he
warn’t no better at the game than you, sir.” Miln sought
the fresh air.

There are at least a dozen suitable ways of interpreting
the name-part in “Mikado.” Quite recently Alan
Morris’s young and festive way came as a revelation, and
Rosevear, at Melbourne Princess’s last week, was another
novelty. Rosevear’s method is the very antithesis of his-
predecessor’s. He is a fat, fatherly Mikado, a genial,
quiet old chap on the surfaft, with a rare taste for
cruelty in an odd corner of his heart. His “ I forget the
punishment for compassing the death of the Heir Appar-
ent” was given with all the eagerness of a wheezy sinner
who wants to think of another naughty story before youi
tear yourself away.

(with heavy costs) for salary from the well-known firm of

fire put an end to that frosty venture, they were dismissed'

SOHO SQUARE, LONDON.
when W. B. Sheridan, many years ago, was touring
Mom-Hand “King Lear” was to be the opening piece In
Certain city. Sheridan, of course, took the King, Monty
Moitlond was Albany, and Harry Saville (he of the eye-
S was Cornwall. Harry and Monty were to dress
together the room assigned them being under the stage.
An iusuection, however, it proved to be so damp and rat-
that thev determined to seek fresh quarters.
V n h v found them in an unused cellar beneath the
orchestra. By some accident, however, they omitted to
tell anyone of the change. On the opening night, Saville
and Maitland both happened to arrive early, and each had
brought a bottle of beer to christen the new den with.
Having dressed, they lit their pipes and opened the bottles.
Presently a few minutes before eight, the orchestra
wfn They heard, and waited. Eight o’clock came,
nfit no call They waited—and the orchestra continued
to scrape. At 8.10, still no call, and the orchestra still
i-nino- strong At 8.15 they concluded that the call-boy
Ct have tied or something, so climbed to the stage
without further waiting. As their heads appeared above
he stage, they heard Sheridan’s fruity voice exclaim:
•■Here the are ;up with the curtain !” They had,
of course, to go on first, and their absence had delayed
the whole show. Things went on pretty right till that
.mint in the first scene where the King, Albany, and Corn-
wall go up stage and leave Cordelia and France in posses-
sion of the front. Then Sheridan opened fire. “Havel
come out here—l6,ooo crimson miles—to have to wait a
,i d half-hour on a couple of scurvy ike you ?
You call yourselves men, you skunks. Why, Ive
seen better men than you made out of paper by a ——
property man.” And so on. As his anger grew with
words Sheridan began to gesticulate madly, and swung
his arms all over the shop. The audience grew interested,
hut' not hearing his words, took his actions for clever by-
nhv and applauded loudly. The reason of the applause
did not strike Sheridan, but Maitland and Saville
<mv it, especially Saville, who rose to the
invasion and, hacking Maitland severely, hissed
“Fawn "you —-, fawn!” and Maitland and he fawned
accordingly. And, as Sheridan went on cursing, those
two wretches punctuated his remarks with fawmngs and
cringings, to the huge delight of the audience. Sheridan,
however thought their actions were in his contempt, and
continued until stopped by bis cue. He never forgave
them. One of the orchestra says it was the funniest thing
he ever saw—the cursing, infuriated Sheridan, the fawning
rogues taking advantage of him, and an audience con-
vinced that the whole incident was a gorgeous slab of
premeditated hy-play. And when the next day’s papers
complimented the three on “an admirable p> ece of
business,” the humour of the thing was complete.
Chas. Wyndham says that through the friction and
mutual ieaiov sies that spring up between stage lovers,
they are rarely friends, but Henry Neville says the true
artist is always as much in love with the heroine as she
will permit him to be.
