Alexander James Scott, sentenced to
death at Auckland (M.L.), for the Waikomiti
murder, is the son of a highly-respected West Vic-
torian timber-merchant. He was always a quiet
unassuming young fellow, and had splendid pro-
spects had he remained in Victoria. The news of
his son’s conviction has nearly killed his aged
father.
Fat man Cleveland, the 18-stone President
of the U.S. (whore, by the way, is his acknow-
ledgement of the effusive congratulations of the
N.S.W. Foreign-trade party ?) “is to be sworn in
on his mother’s little morocco Bible. He was
sworn in on the same Bible in 1885.” This is the
sort of pathos Parkes understands —though, so far
as Parkes’ moral sentiments are concerned, he
would just as soon be sworn in on a copy of the
Decameron, oron a copy of Angus Cameron for that
matter. Cleveland is a favourite with the female
sex by virtue of these little snuffling hypocrisies
and travels largely on hen influence. At the same
time, if that little morocco Bible is put on a table
in front of him, and he has to reach over his
gigantic tummy to lay an impressive hand upon
it, The Bulletin expects to hear by cable that
Cleveland is very ill through violent compression
of the abdomen against the edge of the table, or
something.
At Adelaide, a little while ago, an aborigine
from the Queensland border was brought up on a
charge of murdering another coloured gentleman
from the same locality. The offender had been
dragged 850 miles by a policeman, who, for .about
300 miles of the journey, was kept busy staving off
the relatives of the deceased, who wanted to spear
the captive. There was no coherent evidence, and
the Chief Justice gave it as his decision that the
nigger ought to have been left where he was, to
fight it out with the other party the best way he
could—in other words, that the white man’s law is
a failure. The jury found that the prisoner was
“ unable to understand the proceedings owing to
his ignorance of English,” which was held to be
equivalent to a verdict of “ not guilty.” Then the
Court didn’t know what to do with the prisoner.
He obviously ought to be released, but that would
be tantamount to execution, as the plaintiffs in
the case were waiting for him on the homeward
track ; and he couldn’t be found “ not guilty ” and
executed at the same time; neither could he be
kept in prison for his own safety. So at last the
Court found, by way of compromise, that he was
mentally deranged, and he was sent off to a
lunatic asylum, and the dead man’s family will
wait on the back read in vain.
“The Bulletin’s” Missing
Word Competition.—No. 2.
The number of replies received in the prescribed
time totalled 1303, out of which only one com-
petitor—a Wagga (N.S. W.) lady—was successful
in naming the correct “missing word.” -The
commonest hazard of competitors was “Apostacy,
to which over 100 pinned their faith. Other
favourite guesses were : Weakness, Inconsistency,
Servility, Pusillanimity, Obsequiousness, &c.
“Mrs. K. A. E. Murray, Box 27, P.O., Wagga
Wagga, N.S. W.,” is to be congratulated as
having been the only contestant who chose
“ DUCTILITY,” a guess which is rewarded with
the whole amount received, .£65 3s., a cheque for
which amount has been mailed to the abov e
address.
The Land-Grab Map of Queensland.
The Bulletin departs somewhat from its ordinary
course this week in substituting for an original
cartoon a “Map” (drawn by Mr. H. F.
Hardacre, one of the Queensland Labour
candidates), which the courtesy of the con-
ductors of the Brisbane (Q.) Worker has enabled
us to reproduce. No apology, however, is needed
for giving a wide-spread publicity to a subject of
supreme Australasian importance. The Map is a
most eloquent picture, not only of the battle-
ground of the impending Queensland general
elections, but also ot the monstrous intrigue
against which the workers of the Northern pro-
vince are called upon to fight—the capture by
capitalistic sharks of a third of the whole province,
and practically the control of all the lands at
present unalienated from “the Crown a gigantic
invasion to repel which were worth a Civil War :
The map, remarks the Worker, is neither a jest
nor an exaggeration, but a solemn and earnest levlity.
Under the new Railways Construction (Land Subsidy) Act
of 1892, .passed last session by the most corrupt Parlia-
ment that has dominated Queensland, in violation of its
election pledges, and against the emphatic and repeatedly
expressed wishes of the people, eleven railways have been
approved, and their routes are even now being surveyed
by the agents of the land-grant syndicates at the pro-
vince’s expense, with the object of having the contracts
ratified by the next Parliament.
These eleven railways are as follows:
Mis.
Oharleville to Cunnaraulla *»DOUt 125
Charleville to Thargomindah ... 205
Oharleville to western boundary of province ... ~ 560
Longreach to western boundary of province ... ~ 420
Longreach to Winton » U®
Wiuton to Hughenden ... >■ 140
Hughenden to western boundary of province ... ~ 410
Nornianton to Cloncurry «• n 250
Granite Creek to Georgetown .. ... .. i> 185
Georgetown to Croydon ... .. .. •• »> 84
Gayndah to Degilbo » 40
Total .. .. .. •• 2534
In round numbers 2500 miies of railway costing about
twelve and a half millions sterling. The conditions under
which the railways are to be constructed are briefly :
1. That the railways remain the property oi the syndi-
cate for 50 years when without further purchase they will
become the property of the Government.
2. That they may become the property of the Govern-
ment immediately they are constructed without any pur-
chase except that which has already been made by
grants of land.
3. That they may remain the property of the syndicate
for any period up to 10 years when, in addition to the
grants of land, if the province desires to possess the rail-
ways, it must again pay for them.
If the railways are constructed on condition 1, the
amount of land the syndicates receive may equal in value
but not exceed twice the cost of construction. If on con-
dition 2: An amount equalling once the cost of construc-
tion. If condition 3: Once the cost of construction, and
at the end of 10 years or later, but not previously, a
further payment at their value in solid cash.
It is under either conditions 1 or 3 that the railways will
be constructed (if the people permit it) for no syndicate,
whose sole object is to get as much as possible, will con-
struct railways on condition 2, when under either of the
others it can get twice as much.
The Map represents the amount of land the syndicates
will obtain under condition 1, which is by far the most
likely to be taken advantage of.
Assuming that the 2500 miles of railway will cost £5OOO
a mile, which is a fair assumption, as the average cost of
the province’s railways in the past has been £6917 per
mile—£sooo was the amount assumed by members in the
Assembly during the discussion of the bill and was also
the estimated cost of the Kimberley Transcontinental
Railway from Charleville to Point Parker in 1883—and
estimating the value of land to be given at 2s. 6d. per acre
—MTlwraith’s estimate, and also the estimated value in
the Assembly, while Griffith declared the surface rights to
be worth only a shilling an acre—the total amount of land
to be grabbed by the syndicates wi 1 be 312,500 square
miles, or 200,000,000 acres. This amount the black lines
on the map approximately represent.
But this is not all !“Note with what consummate and
devilish genius the lines are designed to drain awa
from the Government railways the traffic now’ runniny
upon them and to transfer the whole of it over thg
syndicate railways up to the Gulf. It needs but a cone
necting line between Charleville and Longreach when the-
whole traffic of the interior now flowing towards the
coast, along the Southern and Western railways making
Brisbane, along the Central Railw'ay making Rock-
hampton, along the Northern Railway making Towns-
ville, will set in the opposite direction flowing towards
Nornianton. It needs but another connecting line to
transfer this again to Point Parker. All the coastal towns
will sink into insignificant villages and in their place will
rise Point Parker the largest city of Australia ; and the
title-deeds of Point Parker will be owned by the syndi-
cate. Property values will vanish like magic. All the vast
sums of money expended by the province in lighting the
eastern coast, in dredging rivers and constructing har-
bours, wdll have been money thrown aw'ay. While its
railways, upon which £16,000,000 have been spent, will
become mere feeders to the railways of the syndicate,
whose power and wealth and monopoly will overshadow
the land. Three hundred thousand pounds received from
the pastoral tenants annually—and which should be at
least a million—will be transferred into the pockets of the
syndicate. Representative Government also will be gone.
The Judges, the law courts, the local councils and parlia-
ment will be the mere puppets of the syndicate, for it will
buy them all. Freedom of contract would reign in all its
glory—the freedom to accept the syndicate’s terms or
starve.
H.F.H.
A Game Worth Watching.
Dear Bulletin, — Ever since the discontent of
moleskin sent into Parliament a contingent of
lawmakers, the anti-legislative New South Wales
Council has become more so. The cry of the
dwellers in the tents of the Ass has pierced the
slumberous atmosphere of the House of Prey.
The old gentlemen are now moved to stay up as
late as half-past ten of a night to express their
horror and loathing of the unpropertied voter,
the miner who wants to be allowed to mine, and
the tax-collector who would look towards realised
wealth instead of swooping upon poverty and
bluchers. The nominees have shown themselves
to be nominees of the Quadruped, and, with un-
wise heat and candour, have cursed every measure
emanating from the Assembly calculated to secure
better political and social conditions, as a machin-
ation of the ignorant, beer-soaked, covetous
masses.
* * *
They have emasculated the Electoral Bill into
conservative meaninglessness ; they have fired out
the Mining Bill; they are going to reject the
Income tax proposals. They have declared war
to the knife, and the knife to the hilt, against
everything savouring of democratic progress. Con-
sequently, the fingers of Democracy begin to itch
with a great yearning to compress their political
windpipes. Tommy Houghton, M.P., has written
to the papers urging the necessity of sending
into the Council, there to abide for ever
beside the bull - necked and broad - clothed
monopolists, a moleskin contingent who will
do just as the Assembly wishes. George
Reid wants an appeal to the country. - Each of
these proposals may be patriotic—and it mayn’t.
Tommy’s most notable utterance since his appear-
ance in public life was delivered during a troubled
address to his constituents, and consisted in the
simple inquiry, “Was that an egg?” followed by
an immediate vacation of the platform. He may
possibly not care about the bother of re-election,
which a dissolution would involve. Reid, in his
flabby political incertitude, has visions of Premier-
ship. Parkes the party will not have, Bruce
Smith evidently wants to keep to his Bar work,
M’Millan will not enter the lists, and Cash Neild
is an utter and hideous impossibility. Therefore,
Reid might tiptoe into what could hardly, in his
case, be called power, walking circumspectly be-
tween the people and the Ass. A general election
is the only way to the realisation of what would
please Mr. Reid, and wouldn’t do the country
much good.
* * •
It in’t likely that Dibbs will cry havoc and let
slip the horny-handed upon the Council.' He has
shown no inclination that way, and such conduct
would shock many of his friends who move in the
best circles in the United Kingdom. Nor is it
likely that if Reid were in his position he would
do it. What would Wentworth Court, and the
Clubs, and the Charpber of Commerce, and the
Right-thinking Person, and the best-paid cor-
morants of the Civil Service, to which Reid used
to belong, and which put him into Parliament, say
to a proceeding so revolutionary, so subversive
of the Best Interests of the Country ? And
though the multitude count at elections, they
do not count between whiles for much. So
I think it probable that Dibbs will go to the
country, when he can’t much longer help going, on
the Electoral Bill and the other rejected measures,
fulminating against the Council and winking the
other eye. The Opposition will do pretty much
the same thing, accompanied by incidental attacks
on Protection when it gets the chance. Anyhow,
it will take some time to make One-man-one-vote
the law of the land, and I don’t believe that the
leading men in either of the two big parties want
to ruffle the feelings of "the Council, or offend the
moneyed sentiment behind it any more than is con-
sistent with securing and hanging on to the Trea-
sury benches.
* • *
But what I want to specially point out is that
after One-man-one-vote has become law, as it
ultimately must, there is a big difficulty in the
way of its becoming praetice. And I know for
certain that if this difficulty hadn’t been there,
and hadn’t been clearly perceived by many of the
members who voted for equal electoral rights, they
wouldn’t have been so enthusiastic. The outside
Democratic mind appears to be satisfied that
when the Electoral Bill has been agreed to the
matter is practically done with, but a good many
members of Parliament know a lot better than
that. Single electorates with equal voting would,
in the event of the passing of the Local Govern-
ment Bill, kill the roads-and-bridges member.
But local Government is a thing of the remote
future, and the roads-and-bridges members count
on a long life and a merry one before it is accom-
plished. The provisions of the Electoral Bill, as
agreed to in the Assembly, would cut that life
short in most cases, if they could be carried out.
It is only in the sure and certain hope that they
can’t be carried out that they have been agreed to
by many, if not most, of the old members.
# * *
It is over the subdivision of the province into
new electorates on the population basis that the
trouble will arise. This is to be done by a Com-
mission, whose work must be approved of by the
Assembly. And the Assembly largely consists of
members whose political life depends upon not
approving of it.
* * *
The roads-and-bridges member is an abomina-
tion to the land—a public robber, an outrage on
Democracy. He buys support from His consti-
tuents with roads and bridges, and sells his own
support to Governments for the same. He. need
hardly have the ragged remnants of a conscience,
or the vague beginnings of a brain. He may be a
local nonentity, incapable of formulating the idea
of seeking shelter from the fury of the elements,
or a blatant fraud, loaded to the muzzle with
alliterative cant, but his seat is sure while
he can drive a hard bargain with the Govern-
ment of the time for his vote, and in
return secure an unjust local expenditure of
taxes in railways, courthouses, post-offices, gaols
and culverts. Also, he must lay everybody in his
electorate under obligation to him as far as pos-
sible, by personal attention to their grievances, or
aid to their designs upon the general taxpayers.
If he does all this the part he takes in general
lawmaking is a secondary consideration.
# * *
Will the roads-and-bridges member submit to a
subdivision of his electorate which cuts off half
those for whom he has blackmailed Governments
and exploited the public, and gives him in return
strangers who know him not? Will his brother-
member of the next electoratesubmit to an arrange-
ment equally unfavourable to himself? The aver-
age member of any standing has acquired a vested
interest in a certain set of voters, which the alter-
ation of political boundaries would render almost
valueless. Here and there it might happen that
a fertile patch of Orangemen would bo given to
an anti-Homo Rule member in exchange for hostile
Holy Roman voters. But if the Commission does
its work honestly, the reverse effect is just as
likely.to follow Its decision*. What must be par-
ticularly borne in mind is that probably nob
more than one out of ten of the members who
have established a lien on certain electorates
through Treasury-looting in its various forms, or
through any other of the devices of billet-holding,
will be satisfied with the alterations of boundaries
proposed. And what is more certain than that
they will heartily unite on broad, patriotic grounds
in rejecting such proposals, or any other proposal
likely to interfere with their chances of retaining
seats ?
9 * *
I have conversed with more than a dozen
M’s.P. every one of whom voted for “One-man-
one-vote ” and will resist any alteration of the
boundaries of his own stamping-ground. And
every one of these is conscious that to do
so effectively he will have to help others
similarly affected in their resistance. The
question of electoral boundaries is one that
will be log-rolled from the very start, and
probably tossed to and fro for years in the
Assembly. Some of those (I do not refer to new
members, as they have not yet acquired vested
interests in districts) who are loudest in their
anger at the Council’s opposition to the popular
will, are ready to oppose it much more effectually,
and with far less honesty. Because of them, even
though “ One-man-one-vote ” were crammed down
the throats of the grasping nominees to-morrow,
its application would be as far off as before. If
the Labour-party are “lions led by asses,” Demo-
crats who think all the old members were in
earnest when they voted for an equal public
franchise are asses led by Judases, —Yours, &0.,

Extract from an address presented by the
Napier (M.L.) Caledonian Society to Governor
Glasgow :
The society hopes that Maoriland will be one day
governed entirely by Scotsmen, and believes the colony
will then be placed on a footing of commercial pre-
eminence.
It will be a cold day for the man who tries to run
a comic paper, but the trade in religious books and
whisky will be pre-eminent enough.
Pitcairn Island is disappearing under
subterranean volcanic action. Scientists think it
will all have crumbled away in a century.

ing-class misery is simply that the pigs be taught

A Blue Mountains (JSLS.W.) man lately
found, among the leaves of a book that had be-
longed to his deceased mother, a .£5 note issued
by a long-defunct N.S.W. bank, the assets of
which were, years ago, purchased by one of the
“associated” banks. On legal advice, he pre-
sented the “fiver” over the counter of the live
bank. It was honoured. Rummaging through
the departed lady’s little library, he found £BO
more in similar notes which he also turned into
cash.
John M'Elhone, the man with the skull of
Julius Caesar, and the tongue of Bridget Moriarty,
has written to the Sydney press a blistering
thunderblast against the N.S.W. Civil Service. A
bit: —
Only a few days ago, as I was going down Pitt-street,
about a quarter to 3 p.m., I saw two under-secretaries and
a Minister near Tattersall’s Hotel, walking leisurely down
Pitt-street.
What John probably means is that he saw the
Minister and two highly-paid under-secretaries
employing part of their “office-hours” in having
drinks. It is evident that the scorpion-tongued
M‘Elhone has his eye on his old position of Par-
liamentary flagellator and hangman.
Asked by Sydney D.T. interviewer if he
thought “the best Labour representatives came to
that side by conviction against environment rather
than by actual training and experience,” Organiser
Schnadhorst said that “the best Labour repre-
sentative was the man who had actual experience
of the necessities and conditions of labour.” The
reply was as inexplicit as the question. It requires
training and experience to make a man a Demo-
crat “by conviction against environment,”
Poverty of condition is not, as Schnadhorst and
the interviewer seem to think, the school in which
a rock-founded belief in Democratic principles is
engendered. If a poor man be not a sincere
student of social conditions, he is apt to imagine,
on bettering his environment, that poverty is
always a personal fault, and that men should not
blame conditions but themselves. When a man of
this mould gets into Parliament he developes
“ big-head,” and throws Democracy to the winds.
Dear Bulletin, —The N.S.W. railways
are worth, I understand, in round numbers some-
thing over .£40,000,000 sterling, and are paying
good interest on that amount. As you pointed
out last week, they have at present only a fraction
of the traffic which time and settlement will
bring; even supposing that no more money be
expended upon extensions. In a few years’ time
they will be paying double or quadruple the
interest they now yield, and would sell for
000,000 to <£100,000,000. Now, with these
certain prospective gains before them, a syndicate
could afford to offer eighty members of the Legis-
lative Assembly (a substantial majority) a bonus
of <£10,000,000 among them, or £25,000 each,
for getting the little job through the political
machine. I believe there are poor men who have
nothing but their £3OO a year, minus retrench-
ment, who would from a spirit of probity and
patriotism refuse to save themselves from bank-
ruptcy by accepting such bribes. I also believe
there are others who would grab the cash. I am
afraid we shall lose our railways. “Are you in
favour of alienating the tramways or railways?”
should be the first question asked of candidates
at parliamentary elections in every Australasian
province. —W. H. M.

The Melbourne solicitor who is going to
England for a big fortune, recalls another, who
hurried joyfully to take up his Irish estates, but
got no rent, couldn’t sell, and had to come back to
work in Australia.
David Lindsay is about to start out on
an exploring expedition in the interests of a Mel-
bourne syndicate. He travels from Port Augusta
westward until past the West Australian border,
and then turns north through the Murchison
district. He will do a little bit of camel-dealing,
a good deal of prospecting for minerals, and some
land-selection. Elder has nothing to do with the
new scheme.

Henry E. Brougham, who lately died in
Melbourne, was the son of a well-known Aus-
tralian pioneer and a nephew of the celebrated
Lord Brougham. At one time he managed
Ticehurat station, Riverina (N.S.W.)* and was
afterwards a wool-scourer at Wagga.

btmgs has been grossly insulted by an anonymous

but an envelope containing a paragraph cut from
a London paper concerning the suicide of Mr.
Barker, accused of fraudulent banking business.

John Pettie* R;A;* has gone Where the
good painters go; His first famous picture, “An
Arrest for Witchcraft,” hangs in Melbourne
National Gallery, and makes a good contrast to

The late Duke of Marlborouglx was a some-
what intellectual rascal, and in his will he had a
parting slap at his noble order. Thus :“ I dislike
particularly the exclusiveness of family pride, and
I wish not to be buried in the family vault in
Blenheim chapel, but in any suitable place that
may be convenient.” This indifference to the
dust of his ancestors was the most respectable
trait in the wife-kicker’s character.
In O’Shanassy’s day of power in Victoria,
that big man used to be the victim of shoals of
letters of introduction upon the arrival of every
immigrant ship from “th’ ould counthry.” One of
O’Shanassy’s official proteges once got leave to
go down to Sandridge and welcome his immigrant
cousin, of whom, when they met, he presently
enquired—“An’ who gev yeh the letther?”
“ What letther? ” “Why, the letther of recom-
mindash’n to O’Shanassy.” “Shure, Oi have
nayry a letther to him or any wan.” “What !
Yeh’ve no letther ! Thin, what’s the use o’yeh
cornin’ to Osthreelia ? Go back at wanst an’ get a
letther, an’ doan’t attimpt to come here agin
’idout it! ”

The Hebrew is climbing on to the throne.
Ferdinand of Bulgaria, is about to be married to
the gilded daughter of a Jew-banker named
Bleichroder, and as the lady refuses to change
her creed she will be the first royal Jewess in
Europe. The rest is only a matter of time. Out
of this marriage will arise some future King
Moses of Bulgaria, and he will gradually get a
mortgage on the surrounding States and take them
in, till his not very remote descendant Samuel is
one of the Great Powers of Europe.

Some 20 years ago, a man who is now a
Victorian M.P., was local-preaching in an up-
country town where Methodism was epidemic.
Starting from the text —“ Lay ye not up for your-
selves treasures on earth,’’ &c., he used the fact
that bushranger Power was in the neighbourhood
as an illustration of the chances of being robbed,
and ended, by way of contrast: “ But there won’t
be no thievin’ in ’eaven, where the streets are
made of jasper, an’ the mansions are built of gold!
Why, if thieves was allowed hin, they’d be a
pickin’ the streets oop an’ shoovelling away the
treasure!'’ The congregation guffawed irreve-
rently. The preacher, nothing disconcerted,
roared sternly ; “An’ the larfin’shall be turned
to groanin’, an’ there shall be weepin’ an’ gnashin’
o’ teeth!” Which just broke up everything
“right there.”
“Archie” Hall, one of Cobb and Co.’s
coach-drivers of the gold-digging and bushranging
era, is now manager of Wellington (M.L.) tram-
ways. It was he who, in the early days, could
alone get the N.S.W. and Victorian mails through
without accident. When the ’rangers would bail
him up, he always answered their questions truth-
fully—such as when and where he had passed the
gold escort. He never informed on them at head-
quarters, and for this he was once arrested by the
Victorian Government, but on explaining that if
he hesitated about giving the required information,
or reported what gang it was that asked for it, he
never would be able to get the mails delivered,
they let him off. He is an old man now, but
if pushed in the holiday times, or when short-
handed, he will take a tram out himself.

Forty-one years ago four penniless pro-
spectors, Clarkson, Hunt, Cobley and White
found the first gold on the Thames (Maoriland)’
It was a great and wonderful find. The Caledonia
mine alone, on that magnificent field, yielded
about three and a-half millions in gold* and it was
only one—though the richest—among a number of
discoveries. The four prospectors, in a few
months after their strike, were worth £40,000 a
man, and Clarkson’s pickaxe and shovel, which
had turned up the first deposit, were reverently de-
posited in Auckland Museum, where they attracted
attention for many years as the beginning of the
province’s greatness. But a year or two ago an old*
feeble,, discouraged looking man appeared at the'

should be returned to him, as he wanted to go and
potter over the exhausted goldfield for a living,
and was too poor to buy a new set of tools. The
old man was Clarkson. Whether he got his pick
and shovel back isn’t 'recorded. Of his three
mates, White had died mad, Hunt was making a
precarious livelihood prospecting in Australia,
and Cobley was unaccounted for. This sort of
thing is the saddest part of the gold industry.
Victor Daley is on the sick list in Mel-
bourne, and the doctors treat him with more
respect than he would wish for. A poet, not being
a squatter or an opulent stockbroker, regards
medical attention with a suspicious eye.
A lady correspondent of S.M. Herald
points out that 165,138,000 acres of land,
devoted to sheep in N.S.W., worked with a
capital of about =£100,000,000, returned last year
only <£11,036,018, or an average of about Is. 9d.
per acre, whereas all the land under mulberry in
Europe returns an annual average of .£24 per acre.
Further, the annual value of cocoons from the
land under mulberry in Italy alone (an area that
would go into one of the N.S.W. electorates) is
£6,000,000.
IF YOU ARE FOND OF A
Pleasant beverage, Wolfe’s Schnapps will afford
you that gratification, and at the same time in-
vigorate your system and cheer your mind.
“ lb is worth its weight in gold,” is a common
expression. But while the value of gold is easily
affected, the worth of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla, as a
blood-purifier, never depreciates. It eradicates
scrofula from the system.

An instructive

the alleged advan-
tages, in the way
of cheap fares and
freights, to be
gained by selling
the Australian
railways to pri-
vate speculators.
In England, last
year, it was found
necessary to pass
an Act compu'so-
rily reducing the
exorbi tan tc 1 1 arges
for certain classes
of freight on the
railways. The
various compa-
nies, whose com-
petition is sup-
posed to keep rates
down to the lowest
payable level,
some time ago

practically one
happy monopoly,;
hence the trouble.
When the Act
came into force,
the association
promptly raised its
charges about .75

Between Hergott and Faring railway
stations (S. A.) and the Queensland border,
there are 1000 camels employed, and all are
owned and worked by Afghans, of whom 300 are
camped on the Hergott park lands. They pay no
rates, taxes, or rent, and they are driving the
white teamsters right out of the district.
The S.M. Herald is grieved to hear the
death-rattle in the throat of Australian Party-
Government. It mourns the depravity of the
Ministry that can submit night after night to have
its Bills amended in Committee, and that will not
retire whenever a snap majority succeeds in alter-
ing “ doodleumbuck ” to “ doodlenmbang.”

The unconscious growth of Socialistic .ideas
is once more made evident by an article in the
February number of the Nineteenth Century,
entitled “The Doom of tjie domestic Cook,”
wherein C.' S. Layard recommends the erection
of central kitchens to supply food at cost to every
shareholding householder inside their radius,
writer is not apparently a Socialist —he simply sees
the saving of money, time, fuel, service and'food,
the increase of internal and serial'cleanliness that
would result from such a commercial undertaking,
if properly carried on. Half the antagonism to
Socialism is the result of ignorance. .
One of the conspicuous evils of .private
railway ownership is that the railway company
gives large concessions in freight to itt big cus-
tomers, and makes up the loss by charging exor-
bitant rates to the small ones. For instance, in
England, the big grain-importers, who segd mariy
thousands of tons annually over the rails, have
their goods conveyed at about half the rates
charged to the local farmer, which practically
means that Britain subsidises the foreign producer
to help him to wipe out the native agriculturist.
British railways exist almost entirely for the
benefit of the importer, nowadays.
Mr. J. G. Griffin, the new Parliamentary
candidate for Canterbury, N.S.W., once over-
whelmed Parkes with the magnificence of his
apparel. He was electioneering then, too, but as
the prospective constituents were mostly Free-
traders he called at Parkes’s private house for a
letter of introduction to the chief magnates of the
far-off bush electorate. Parkes complied, but
declares that he spoke only a few words to the
canninatp, being spellbound at the gorgeousness
ot the spectacle. Later, Parkes was sorry that
he gave the letter, as the political Beau Brummel,
on rejection in the country, abjured Freetrade
and assaulted St. Leonards as a thorough-going
Protectionist. " *
Some Australian constituencies are said to
believe that they would have a better grip on their
representatives if the constituency paid them and
not the State. That’s true, but it wouldn’t work.
In the first place, the wealthy man would always
be able to bribe the meaner and more ignorant
souls by offering his services gratis; secondly,
their representative would find some difficulty
after a time in living on voluntary contributions
which no one had time to collect and few would
pay.

Patriotic League. At the recent meeting one of
the latter fearlessly.asserted bis determination to

silver. Under these conditions, the young man
ingenuously pointed out, “ shareholders of the
Broken Hill mines would divide <£2,250,000 a
year more in dividends.” And nobody thought
of mentioning that, if this statesmanlike principle

every scrip'-holdfer-in Newcastle a .Jay Could. In
fact, on the same principle, the whole nation may
become inatari tly.atfd .fabulously wealthy any day
by legislating its soil up to =Bl per square inch.

The Bulletin's London correspondent) now begi'p

movement in England, and to' admit that a new
power has been created which will be tremendously
felt at next general election. It has-been practi-
cally decided by the Labour leaders that" wherever
there is a,vacancy, either- municipal or parliamen-

contested the election, the latter, .although bit-
terly'opposed by the Liberals,’ came .within a
thousand votes or so'of the Tbr.y. Tils Liberal
ppposition bas given much dissatisfaction to the
“ horny-hahded,” 'who is determined to show tha,t
he will no longer submit to be the mere cat’s-paw
,of‘his quasi friends, who simply make use of him
when his vote'is wanted, and kick him off when
.lisas no longer of service. If, in future, the Liberals
want Labour .co-operation, it must be upon some-
thing like equal terms,, and wherever the Labopr
vote is' the stronger, there it must have direct
representation. Failing this, the Liberal. party
will be effaced. Out of the tactics taught by Par-
nell will come the British revolution.
-Constipation, or costiveness, is qpe of the
most frequent results of liver complaint, and re-
quires prompt and careful Attention, for if the
fecal matter becomes impacted in the bowels
dangerous effects are to be apprehended. The
justly’celebrated Dr. Abernethy said that inatten-
tion to the action of the stomach and’ bowels was
the cause of ninety per cent, of our physical ail-
ments; and modern science has proved this asser-
tion correct. Care, however, requires to be
(observed in the choice of remedies, for (here
‘are numerous drastic and violent cathartics sold
under various fancy names, but Dr. Fletcher’s
Pills are used with the best advantage in such
cases. These pills are from the prescription of
Dr. Bell Fletcher, of Birmingham, England, arid
were used by him in his private and General
Hospital practice there for many years with the
greatest success. This, as a matter of course,
proves.their reliability, and Mr. A. M. Beau-
eharrjp, Rata, Rangitikei,: N.Z., says:—“ I wish
to inform you of the good Fletcher’s Pills are
doing here. Everybody! uses them, arid speaks
well of them ; and so do I, for they are the best I
ever used in my life. ’, They can be obtained
from all medicine vendors, or direct from F. M.
Clements, Newtown, Sydney, for thirteen penny
stamps. •- „

First there came the announcement, per
cablegram, that Queen Victoria, or somebody, was
anxious for certain Australian horse-marines to
form part of the Royal escort at the opening of
the Imperial Institute. Then the Victorian
War Minister telegraphed an earnest appeal that
the said warriors might be allowed to chip in.
Finally came the glad tidings that the “ Imperial
authorities,” had sanctioned an Australian addi-
tion to the show “ in compliance with the sugges-
tion made by the Vic. Defence Department.”
The weaving of each silken bond is an interesting
study.
• • •
Queen Victoria’s “drawings from nature,”
exhibited in Melbourne, are not quite as weak as
could be, but they are rather ghastly things to
inflict upon her distant possessions. The most
shocking of them is a sketch of Prince Arthur,
perpetrated 40 years ago. Some sketches of
female grandchildren, dated 1875, bear a general
resemblance to the Royal Family, and suggest
that the august artist improved as she grew older.
The Queen’s colouring is primitive. A splodge of
red does duty for a maiden’s blush, and a brushful
of morning chocolate expresses a head of hair.
Several royal etchings had not been hung at time
of writing, possibly because Josh. Lake dreads
the consequences. Already the aforesaid portrait
of little Arthur begins to excite ridicule, and
doubts concerning the wisdom of a ruler who
could exhibit such puerility in public are ex
pressed unsparingly. It is a sort of reflection
on the Litany. Nobody can pray with gusto for
the Sovereign who takes little Arthur seriously.
• • •
Should Makin and his wife be hanged
together (unless an appeal to the Privy Council be
permitted the male Makin will almost certainly be
executed), the event will be the principal gallows
sensation since the hanging of the Mannings, 40
years ago.
• • •
There can be no gainsaying the fact that
Justice Stephen, in the emotional gem of prepared
rhetoric with which he overwhelmed the miserable
baby-farmers whom he was sentencing to the
gallows, took a great deal for granted. When he
eloquently depicted the heart-cries of the mothers
bereft of their offspring—the lamentations of
social trespassers who only sought to hide away
their “accidents” with the least expense and
publicity ! —and pictorially denounced the fiends
who would bury a dead baby in a backyard “as
you would the carcase of a dog,” he certainly
abandoned judicial certainties for hasty and
imaginative conclusions. An equally imaginative
historian might as easily conjure up in the dock
an exaggerated Makin who should, with hardened
gall, interrupt the Judge in his vision of judg-
ment by snapping out—“ What are you giving
us? How d’ye know we didn’t bury that
kid alive?” How indeed? There was no
more real legal evidence to show that some or all
of the missing babies were not buried alive, than
there was to show that all of them had not died a
natural death, and that the Makins had only been
guilty of concealment of birth, in order to save
registration worries and funeral expenses.
# # *
One of the solemn humours of the final
stages of the Makin trial was contributed by
Attorney Tommy Williamson when addressing
Judge Stephen as legal adviser for the prisoners
(as has become the privilege, under the new
N.S.W. Act, of a mere unbewigged attorney). In
his best municipal style, Mr. Williamson thanked
the Court for the impartiality ; which it had
exhibited throughout the trial! How disgusted
Judge Stephen was !
• # #
Makin himself is a psychological conun-
drum. The most unexpected extremes meet in
his publicly-seen character. ”Sln this Herod of the
Sydney suburbs the most -wildly expectant of
philosophic analysts would never have expected
to find any qualities resembling, in the faintest
degree, either heroism or the chivalric instinct,
Yet both were evinced,'in some of their unusual
? bases, during the period of Makin’s public torture,
'he man absolutely compelled the admiration of
the Crown Law officials by the dogged determina-
tion with which he maintained a bold front while-
the halter was being drawn around his neck.
When at last he was absolutely trapped,
when even the off-chance of a successful technical
appeal was changed to a bitter certainty of awful
menace, when Judge Stephen’s avalanche of
rhetoric was hurled upon him and his hysterical
partner, Herod Makin put his arm around his
wife’s waist and tender!}' sustained her in the hour
of her darkest adversity ! Who can read the com-
plex riddle of a criminal heart? ”
* « *
And Mrs. Makin, who had made her living
out of baby-fanning, when she at last stood in the
shadow of the noose and beam, she moaned :
‘‘My babes! my babes!” Which babes?—the
ones she murdered or the ones she had borne to
Herod? Probably she meant Herod’s, liven the
tigress likes its own cubs.

A box containing the clothes of Emily
Mather (Deeming’s late cemented relative) goes
to England by the steamer Habsburg, consigned
to the mother of the murdered woman. The
Bulletin is prepared to take reasonable odds that
some calamity will overtake the Habsburg. The
curse of Deeming is on board, and, judged by
recent events, that curse is just beginning to get
into good working order.
* * *
The logical result of the testimonial of
character gushingly given in the press by &
good-natured Sydney clergyman to the unfortu-
nate young man wrongfully arrested on sus-
picion of having murdered his fiancee, and since
honourably discharged* should be certificates like
this: “This is to say that in my opinion
John Smith, a constant attendant at my Little
Bethel Bible-class and a diligent searcher after
Truth, would never, under any circumstances,
ravish and murder his sweetheart.” Who shall
draw the line between ambidexter and sinister
friendship? The best certificate of a man’s
character is sometimes his face. The remark-
able narrowness of the jaw possessed by the
small, frail and attenuated J eater certainly offers
to anybody but the most dunder-headed policeman
irrefragable proof that he would be no more likely
to conceive or carry out sq abominable an outrage
than would the waxen fisrnre of old Johnny
Fawkner in Kreitmayer’s show. The Bulletin
always sympathises with ' the man whose good
character is vouched for by the clergy. The cloth,
no doubt, means well, but in its innocence it has
certified to the bona-fides of so many plausible
rascals that a testimonial from a minister of the
Gospel is one of the very last things ambitioned
by anybody who knows his way round.

The position of N.S.W. Acting-Governor
Darley is pathetic. His term of vice-regal gran-
deur lasts but for a few weeks, and, having an
economic soul, he didn’t take possession of Govern-
ment House, which lies temporarily deserted.
But through the horrid irony of fate it seems just
possible that a wandering Austrian Archduke will
strike the province during the Chief Justice’s spell
of transient pomp. If so, his Honor will have to
go to the expense of moving into Government
House, so that he may more impressively enter-
tain a potentate who is reputedly one of the
hardest men on earth to entertain. He will
have to cart his plate and linen to his new
domicile, and probably to borrow more plate
and linen to meet the sudden strain on his
resources. Then he will have to read up Austrian
court etiquette and spend an agonising fortnight
living up to it.
Austrian court etiquette is about the toughest
ever heard of. In the whole two weeks
the Archduke mustn’t see Mr. Darley’s
back once, therefore the Acting-Governor, when
he wants to quit the room, must bob out back-
wards, and if there is anything in the way he will
inevitably fall over it. It is an open question
whether he can sit down without permission,
and if the Archduke forgets to give the
required permission he may have to stand
up for three days and nights at a stretch.
When he has been allowed to sit down it is ques-
tionable whether he can get up again without the
Archduke’s authority. He will probably be re-
quired to speak German whether ho knows it or
not. When he advances to meet the Archduke
they must both take exactly the same number of
steps, and arrive on the same leg right in the cen-
tre of the room ; if this manoeuvre isn’t conducted
successfully they have to go back and do it all
over again. If the Archduke wants to go out at
night in disguise and have a fly round in China-
town, etiquette demands that the Roman-faced
Chief Justice (a shining light of the Y.M.0.A.)
shall accompany him, whether upright or on all
fours we really do not yet know. There must be
a separate entertainment at Government House
every day, and it must be sufficiently novel and
cheerful to lift the horribly-dejected heir (who
became heir because of the giddiness, ending in
suicide, of the Emperor’s son) out of his habitual
melancholy, and bring a look of joy to his dreary
countenance.
By the time the visit is over, Chief _ Justice
Darley will not only have “ blewed ” his hard-
earned savings, but his constitution will be all
broken up with riot and nocturnal dissipation,
and Government House will be one mass of cigar-
ash and stale tobacco-srnoke and assorted ruins.
Then the Acting-Governor will just have time to
get the place cleaned up for his successor and get
back into private life. His reign will be short
and full of trouble, and the memory of his visitor
will haunt him for the balance Of his days, and in
his dreams he will walk backwards before spectral
archdukes for myriads of ages, and prance in a
court suit over the entire solar system, with his
sword getting between his legs all the while.

Captain Taylor, M.L.A., mentions, with
virtuous pride, that the expenses of the Melb.
Mace Board did not exceed .£2O. Evidently the
lady witnesses, one of whom looked very thirsty,
were not invited to take refreshments at the
country’s cost. And Taylor, too, must have sold
his valuable time at an alarming sacrifice.
* # #
In the opinion of the Melb. Mace Board
the missing bauble was “ stolen for the purpose of
being melted down for its value, and, notwith-
standing the continued efforts of the detective-
police for 15 months no trace of it has been
found,” If the Board’s opinion were melted down
for its value it probably wouldn’t be found worth
the paper it was written on.
# # *
Meanwhile, The Bulletin, after careful
consideration, finds as follows : “That the Mace
was not stolen. It strayed. And in consequence
of the search made by the detective-police all trace
of it was lost in the melting-pot. And that the
falsehood of one or more witnesses who gave
evidence before the Mace Board has been reck-
lessly assumed by the said Board.”
* * #
When the floods had just subsided a num-
ber of Brisbane ladies formed themselves into a
kind of Dorcas Society, and met together on cer-
tain days to make impossible undergarments for
the washed-out sufferers. They had no particular
experience at the work, and the results didn’t
amount to much ; they stopped to talk too fre
quently, or to argue about a pattern, or to advise
each other concerning a gusset or something, and
the whole meeting put together didn’t do enough
to earn a living for two or three ordinary seam-
stresses. Nevertheless, the Flood Committee
felt that the Samaritans wanted something to keep
them up, and every working day a comfortable
lunch and afternoon tea were sent in and charged
to the fund, and the cost thereof made the Dorcas
Society a dead loss. At last, a more democratic
woman than the rest put her small kid boot down
on the extravagance, and the refreshments were
abolished. After which the Dorcas Society faded
away till there was practically nothing left of it.

The fact.that the above-mentioned individuals
have given certain sums in charity is no doubt
interesting, but when it is published every day for
a week or two, the interest dies away a good deal,
and people besgin to wonder whether the ad. goes
in free, or whether it is paid for out of the fund, or
how ? Moreover, John See and John M’Farlane
are both “ hon. treasurers,” and their signatures
are on the advertisement, and it doesn’t look well
to see any man advertising his own donation and
that of his friend Jersey 18 times or thereabouts,
when the subscription of the ordinary citizen is
only inserted once. The Bulletin hereby invites
Mr. See'and his friend to stop blowing about their
donations quite suddenly.

Dear Bulletin, —Your little “ love-story”
about the lady whose hand was much sought in
marriage on account of her many “mishaps,” is,
in reality, founded on fact. In Spencer’s “Study
of Sociology ” it is pointed out that the old Poor
Laws created a class of women valued as wives on
account of the number of their illegitimates. The
difference between fiction and fact is simply that
between paternal and State contributions. It is
reminiscent of the charade said to have been
played by French children. The lover went away
to make his fortune in the Southern Seas, and
returned, after nine years’ absence, with nine bags
of gold to his faithful betrothed. Repressing her
emotion, she draws a curtain and reveals nine
children—one to match each year and rnoney-bag
saying, proudly: “I, too, have not been idle.”
The question now arises as to whether all such
exhibitions of industry and thrift do not entitle
the lady-possessor to At least one vote. I await
a reply from N.S.W. senator Jacob.—Yours, M.P.

The people who will be chiefly affected by
the reduction of the Argus are those who have
taken the Age in the past and been ashamed of it.
Loyalty to their principles is thus priced at one
penny per day. Ask such persons for a news-
paper, and you would be handed one with the
apologetic remark, “ It’s only the Age ! ”

TX/"ANTED, a GOVERNESS, for the country, to

(organ and piano), singing, dancing, drawing and

If the salary is to be moderate the advertiser’s
requirements are certainly not. What sort of a
golden dustman must it be who wants four brats
taught English, French, piano and organ, singing,
dancing, drawing and painting, all the expensive
extras of a private school for—a “ moderate ”
salary ?

A nice little bit of swamp landscape en-
livened by cows, is exhibited in the Australian
Room at Lake’s show (Melb.). “Morning, at
Heyiugton,” by John Sefton, price 4 guineas.
One of these days the artist may get 40 guineas
for pictures with no more Work in them, and in
the meantime he sets a good example to his
brethren who have exaggerated notions of their
market value. “Morning, at Heyiugton,” is
“ sold,” as it jolly well ought to be.
» * •
Prices put upon some of the British
pictures, in the adjoining rooms, suggest a
splendid margin of “com.” on the possible sale.
As much as £l5O is asked for “October,” art
unattractive pot-boiler by Waterldw, A.R.A.,
and other things, with smaller names on them,
seem to have been painted specially for Josh.
Lake to take to Australia, These are not pot-
boilers —which is an honourable term, after all—
but out-and-out flat-catchers, travelling on their
good old British impudence.
A DOG AND A WIFE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Brown had a dog—a mongrel from the gutter—
Whose sly demeanour argued theivish ways ;
Yet Brown could never once be brought to utter
One word concerning him except in praise.
The dog was faithless - following nasal leading
To foreign larders, owned by other men ;
Yet Brown was constantly his mongrel feeding,
And sought him daily in his canine den.
When neighbours urged the frequent misdemeanour-
The slaughtered poultry and the worried cat— *
Brown’s sense of chivalry at once grew keener,
IHs dog, he vowed, could never have done that.
The cur was fond of heeling passing horses j
A man unseated was a rapture rare ;
He revelled hourly in his evil courses,
And died—suspended by a rope —in air.
Brown’s dog is gone ; but Brown is daily lying
About the mongrel and his virtues high—
And all the friends who sped the canine dying,
Are hoping daily that Brown, too, may die.
Brown had a wife, a modest, gentle lady,
Who did her duty, as some women do ;
But Brown, himself, was just a trifle shady,
And donned his virtue when he went to woo.
Each found the other not the sort of being
Each had imagined in the courting days,
For Mrs. Brown, Brown’s faults could not help seeing
And Brown said nothing in his lady’s praise.
Poor Mrs. 8., from Brown’s cold persecution,
No harbour of tranquillity could reach,
She bore her woes with silent resolution,
For Brown claimed full monopoly of speech.
Among his mates Brown gave his free opinion
The wife should always be the household slave,
Nor should she long to exercise dominion
In any sphere this side the yawning grave.
Brown’s wife is dead—his callous conduct filled her
With suicidal mania at the end ;
And after he was certain he had killed her,
He wooed and won her most familiar friend.

WOLFE'S SCHNAPPS
Is not only a superb stomachic, but stimulates
both the organs of urination and the bowels to
vigorous action ; avoid imitations.
John Bull’s gold or silver coin is, as often
as not, left to speak tor itself by its circumference.
The old spade-guinea on John Bull’s watch-chain
has no value stated upon either side, neither has
the ordinary half -crown, nor the Jubilee sovereign.
Even the latest edition of the shilling has to be
taken for granted. Good old John Bull! lie has
learned to recognise his coinage by its variations
in size and “tail ” design, and the other nations of
the earth are not worth considering in the matter.
Now, if England were only Scotland the cautious
Mint wouldn't dream of issuing a sovereign with-
out a footnote to the effect that it is worth one
pound sterling or 4SO bawbees.

KENT STREET, SYDNEY.
The Bishop of Adelaide relates how he
Was so pleased with the singing of his cathedral
choir, at some special week-day service, that he
“tipped” the boys to stimulate them to fresh
exertions. But, when the evening service came,
he repented of his donation. Each boy-Jack in
the chorus had a pound of “cocoa nut chips ” on
his chest, and the central solo sounded like a bull-
frog suffering from the death-rattle.
Lord Hopetoun contributed £5 towards a
presentation to a Melbourne policeman whom he
had frequently met on duty at Government House,
where he “ formed a high opinion of his services.”
Which looks as though a good deal of judicious
“chuckingout ”is necessary at vice regal festivals.

“spooning.” Suddenly the girl exclaims:

Dear Bulletin, —“ Snakes” Ellis, referred
to by “Gi ” in a late issue, and a member of the
now much-advertised Howard do Walden family,
was a snake-fancier ere ever he saw the Barcoo.
At Sandhurst (Eug.) military college, in ’63, the
one-armed veteran Capt. Woolhouse had once to
demand this explanation from an orderly of C
company: “How is this, Allan: Mr. Ellis tells
me you haven’t been near his room for days?”
“No, I haint, sir; it’s a-swarmin’ alive with
deadly reptiles.” Captain Woolhouse went to
reconnoitre, but closed Ellis’s door with a pre-
cipitancy unusual to a brave man so soon as he
saw several writhing serpents playing hook-and-
ringbolt games among themselves. Ellis’s snakes
were banished beyond the precincts of the college,
quarters being provided for them at the “ Duke of
York.” —Yours, Cq.

—Up our way (a N.S.
Wales district) a
clergyman was stuck
badly in the “offer-
tory.” He wrote to
the lady-head of every

her that he had a
foundling to baptise ;
would she charitably
permit the waif to be
christened at her re-
sidence? If so, he
intended to name it
after her; also, would
she and her good man
stand sponsors? Then
he carted a borrow-
ed baby around the
district to eacli house
consecutively. There
was a gush of re-
sponsive charity.
Each complimented
woman cordially
agreed with the sug-
gestion that she should
give a guinea to her
namesake, and each
made more or less of
a cake in honour of
the event. Result ; A
baby with 26 distinct
sets of names ; 641 b.
of uneaten cake ; and
£27 6s. in hard cash.

* # #
Melb. County Court considers that the
relations between Mr. and Mrs. Clara Parker are
“of a most remarkable character.” Husband and
wife each put in claims for money lent to the
other, and this in quite a pleasant, connubial way.
There seems to be no real animosity between the
accommodating couple. Parker states, more in
sorrow than in anger, that prior to 1890, fickle
Clara went away to America (quite unexpected
like) for one year. “When she came back she
owed him £4OO, and repaid him £375,” for which
amount she ineffectually sued her lovin’ husband
last week. Clara was under an impression that
the said repayment was a loan, the misunder-
standing having arisen from the slovenly manner
in which the receipt was drafted, we presume.
The Court was right. Parker and his Clara, con-
sidering their marriage relations, are remarkable
characters, indeed. And where does millionaire
Joe Clarke come in ?
A Modern Miracle-Worked
On Saturday bight, “Professor” Richard, the
“electric healer” opened his show at Sydney
School of Arts, admission varying from 3s. down-
wards. There Was a large audience, and the
takings evidently dropped like the gentle dew
from heaven into the good man’s bank-account.
The “ Professor what he is professor of does
not appear very distinctly—was preceded by u
tired-looking man who Bounded on a somewhat
discordant piano With the groan of a lost soul in
its lower notes, and as the frowsy curtain went up
this person assaulted his instrument to the tune
of “Seethe Conquering Hero Comes.” Then he
came. On close inspection the “Professor”
proved to be a greasy-lookiug, intensely cocky
little man, a caricature in petto of the departed
flautist Giammona, with a vast bush of black hair
and a curious, egg-shaped, yellow countenance.
He talks in shattered English, and in a pious
strain of broken snuffle, and most of his parts of
speech are crippled in both legs. Also, he has a
moist, thick voice, and alludes frequently to his
“ojence.” His countenance though this is
entirely a side-issue and has nothing to do with
the subject—is strikingly like that of a certain
oily foreigner who used to sell ladies’ gloves in
Melbourne. This artist invented a new way of
fitting on his wai’es: the girl extended her hand with
her elbow resting on the counter, and the oily one
gently stroked her fingers with his podgy hand
and insinuated the glove into its place by the aid,
apparently, of an electric current. And, as he did
so, he used to leer upwards into the customer’s
eyes with his huge head in a capsized position,
and say, softly and lovingly: “Is tha-a-at ni-i-i-ica
easy way, ladee?” But, as we have said, this is a
mere digression.
* * *
On Saturday night the “Professor” came to
the front in a long black coat, and for twenty-five
weary minutes he told a dislocated tale about an
A CADAVER.
MELBOURNE MAN : “But then, you see, Melbourne is so well laid out.”
SYDNEY MAN : “Yes, so well laid out that it’s a pity they don’t give it
decent burial.”
absolutely incomprehensible female who seemed
to have no possible bearing on anything. As far
as could be made out, this lady ran away from
home at the age of 17, and went somewhere, and
an altogether undefined and shadowy man settled
■£sooo on her, whereupon she came back from the
place she went to and married another man still
more undefined, who took her £5OOO, and a third
man came on the scene and insisted on her going
to an hospital under some horrible circumstances
not clearly specified, and when she came out and
got clear of the fangs of the third man, the second
man had vanished and the house was “for let,”
and the first man was never again heard of. “We
shall commit a fall,” said the Professor, wildly,
“ and is it any reason that there shall be one lost
sheep and we shall throw dust on that sheep?”
For 25 minutes the Professor rushed along con-
cerning this female and the three men in a dust-
storm of adjectives and broken participles and a
blizzard of decomposed interjections, and then the
yells of the audience broke him up, and the story
of the interminable lady ended abruptly in some
touching remarks about the British flag.
* # «
Then the healing began. The first patient was
a deaf lady, who made no response when her ear
was yelled into. Plainly she was a bad case.
After the Piofessor had charged himself with
electricity by jerking at a handle, and had done
sundry performances which were rather hard to
follow owing to his furious velocity, the patient
was put up at the centre of the stage while the
healer asked her a few questions about her fami.y
affairs from the wings. This was evidently in-
tended to show that she had improved con-
siderably, but as the patient’s replies were
practically inaudible, no one could tell with
certainty whether she heard anything or not.
Even in the front row it was difficult to say
whether she was giving a few extracts from
an Vet of Parliament, or whether she war
simply scared, or whether she was cursing
the Professor, or what. Presently that gentle-
man handed over her bonnet and showed her
off the platform. The next patient was also a
lady, and she stated th it she had been deaf for 30
years. She was also understood to be partially
blind in one eye. This patient, however, an-
swered with reasonable success when the Pro-
fessor yelled an interrogation into her ear, where-
upon he didn’t yell any more. When her cure
was over for the night she answered questions
addressed to her at three or four yards range with
tolerable coherence —that is to say, with about the
same coherence as she did when yelled at before
the cure. Neither she nor the first patient stated
definitely that she heard any better after th
treatment, and nothing was said about any im-
provement in her eye. She also took her bonnet
and left the platform in a stunned and bewildered
fashion amid tremendous applause. Just here
the Professor made a few scientific remarks about
what he called “Anna-Tommy.”
* m *
The third subject was said to have become
blind “on the sth of March,” from which it
seemed as if the affliction had been of an instan-
taneous kind. The cause of this sudden calamity
was not stated, nor was it explained whether the
blindness was total or only partial. When the
treatment was over the patient located a burning
match held close to his eyes, and identified one or
two colours at close range, though with some diffi-
culty; but whether he could or could not have done
the same on his first appearance was not
explained. Nobody seemed to notice this point,
however, and the applause was terrific. The
Professor, by this time, had evidently got the
audience by the hair, and he complacently dabbed
his smoking countenance with his handker-
chief. The man who perspires profusely
usually has plenty energy. This is a
generalisation—perhaps it is a platitude. The
blind man departed without any apparent
enthusiasm in his sad, lack-lustre countenance.
He didn’t say that he was better, or worse, or just
the same ; he had to be assisted off the stage very
much as he was assisted on to it, and he looked
as little like a cured patient as could well be
imagined. After him a semi-paralytic ex-police-
man was helped into the chair, and partially un-
dressed behind a curtain, and after a few further
remarks about Anna-Tommy the Professor tackled
him manfully. This sufferer looked healthy, and
didn’t seem very paralytic. When the operation
was over he walked once or twice across the stage,
and departed—with a little assistance. After him
came the triumph of the evening—a rheumatic
{jatient who arrived with apparent difficulty, and
eft at a gallop with his legs bare and his boots in
his hand. The excitement over his exit was a
fearful thing to witness. When he was gone the
healing exhibition was over for the night.
# • *
Each of these patients brought a letter from a
clergyman, magistrate, or J.P., vouching for his
or her genuineness. The first three of these letters
were examined with special care by the present
writer, and they presented this curious difference ;
two clergymen set forth that “ the bearer is suffer-
ing,” &c., while the third, a J.P., merely re-
marked, in a non-committal fashion, concerning
the paralytic person, that “ the bearer in-
forms me he is suffering.” Possibly if the writers
had all three been J.’sP., they would all have set
forth that the bearer had only “informed ” them ;
the magistrate, as a rule, has a mind that ie
rendered move or less cautious by contact with
lawyers, cronk witnesses and hog-thieves, while
the parson is so accustomed to vouching for
miracles which occurred centuries before he
was born, and swearing to things which he
never saw, that he grows somewhat reckless, and
is about the loosest witness in the community.
Owing to the persistent discord of the piano it
was impossible to know whether the patients
made any remarks, favourable or otherwise, to the
Professor while under treatment. Also, there was
nothing of a visible or definite character to con-
nect the patients mentioned in the letters with
those upon the platform. When the curtain went
down the rheumatic person already mentioned
appeared suddenly at the back of the hall, where
he had evidently been putting on his boots and
socks in retirement, advanced at a vigorous run,
and bounding on the stage vanished behind the
curtain. Whether he wanted to say anything of a
private nature to the Professor, or whether
he had been shoo’d off the platform in such
haste that he had left some needful garment
behind him and he couldn’t go home with-
out it, didn’t appear. But considering the
decisive manner in which the healer bounced an
unauthorised patient who tried twice to get on the
stage without the necessary introductions, this
undue familiarity on the part of the rheumatic
person was surprising, to say the least of it.
• * •
There is much hard coin of the realm in the
business, anyhow, and electric healing, done gratia
in a big hall, pays a lot better than the same thing
done in a consulting-room for a two-guinea fee.
Also, there is a certain amount of humour incidental
to the business, as witness the following extract
from a Melbourne Standard report, quoted with
all due solemnity on the “ Professor’s ” circular ;
She was carried up by four attendants, like an over-
grown Queen of the May. She was placed behind a
screen while her clothes were being adjusted for treat-
ment, and when the screen was removed and she con-
fronted the audience, the spectacle was a strange one.
Her legs were absolutely bared. She had not stood for
18 months, and was recommended by the Mayor of
Richmond. In a few minutes she was walking up and
down the stage, having even put on her own boots and
stockings.
Considering this and the hideously inconsequential
story—strangely reminiscent of Mr. F.’s aunt—
about the lady who was taken to the hospital,
The Bulletin suspects the hairy wonder of being
a person who jokes with much difficulty.
The three portraits attached represent
Emma Harrison, the victim of the recent Darling-
hurst tragedy ; Jeater, her fiance, originally
arrested on suspicion, bub subsequently dis-
charged ; and Archer, now in custody. Miss
Harrison, during the temporary absence of the
other inmates of the house, was outraged and
strangled, the crime being only discovered on the
ensuing morning. Jeater had been with her
from eight o’clock till ten, and, about an
hour after his departure, it is alleged,
screams were heard issuing from an upper room.
The accused, Archer, who lived in the house,
is, apparently, unable to account for his move-
ments on the night in question, except by stating
that he fell asleep somewhere in a state of intoxi-
cation. Also, sundry blood-stains were found oh
his clothing, and on these facts the case for the
prosecution mainly rests up to date.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The City and County I. L. and B. Co. of
Sydney seems likely to break the record by paying
nothing in the £ whatsoever* Some time ago, when the
Oity and County began to get into serious trouble, the
directors had an inspiration, by virtue of which they
borrowed £22,000 to go on with from the Colonial Finance
Co., at 14 per cent, per annum (nine per cent, interest
and five per cent* commission), and gave as security a
mortgage over all the company's assets and the uncalled
capital as well. And now the assets have shrunk so
much, and the uncalled capital is so very uncertain, that
no one can say positively that the Colonial Finance Co.
won’t mop up the whole concern, deposits, capital,
and all. The Colonial Finance Co. itself has had bad
times, but it has kept its nose to the sea and been steered
by people who were on deck all the while.
* * •
The Victorian Mortgage and Deposit Bank,
(Melb.) is being calmly and peacefully liquidated by two
gentlemen who decline to have “ anything to do with the
press.” M‘AJlister Howden, forulerly of the Mercantile
Finance crowd, and J. S. Mercer* a faithful relative of
Matthew Davies, enjoy the winding-up of this delectable
“bank.” Last week they called a Confidential meeting of
depositors to hear the said M‘Allister tead a report in an
unobtrusive manner. Also, the liquidators sought
authority to effect compromises, and the Unfortunate
meeting gave them authority to do any mortal thing
which would tend to produce the ghost of a dividend. A
suggestion that a copy of the official report should be sent
to the newspapers was indignantly sat upon by the liqui-
dators, but they consented to have it printed for private
circulation. This was kind ; nevertheless, the report will
not prove a very useful addition to financial scandal
literature when it gets into print. The unwritten history
of the Victorian Mortgage and Deposit Bank would be
much more interesting.

Re State concessions granted, as is usual in
N.S.W., without the stipulation of any penalty for non-
fulfilment. It is quit? common to see a private bill sent
to a select committee, then debated and amended by both
Houses, and finally passed, only to be afterwards dropped
by the promoters, simply because they fail to float a com-
pany or borrow money for the construction of work. In
the case of the Darling River water scheme, the £lO,OOO
bond insisted upon quickly killed the whole thing. The
promoters of any “private enterprise” who can’t or
won’t put up a reasonable penalty for breach of contract,
should not be allowed to waste the time of Parliament.

The Sydney raining markets are in a pitiable
condition. It is a boom or nothing in Sydney. The
speculators, investors, and jobbers of Melbourne and
Adelaide do keep stocks alive, and negotiate a hundred
transactions to one completed in Sydney. Whatever it
may be in other directions, Sydney is the slowest mining
community on earth.
# * #
The new financial man of Sydney D.T., refer-
ii- g to the immorality of “underwriting ” in floating
o nnpanies, remarks that the system is carried too far, but
wants to know “ how it is possible to draw the line.” The
writer knows not the many mysterious details of London
underwriting, but has observed its effects on the mining
industry to which it is invariably applied, and would sug-
gest that the line be drawn through it altogether. It is
t tc concealment of underwriting and the over-capitalisa-
tion which underwriting involves that makes it wholesale
robbery of the investing public. The underwriting sys-
t m even damned back for three years the progress of the
Randt, now the greatest gold field in the world. Every-
thing can be bought too dearly ; in the natural order of
things it is the public that loses what the underwriter
gains.

Because of their silence on the subject we have
it published that there is no urgency for the associated
Banks of N.S.W. to proclaim their determination to stick
io each other in distress, as has been done in Melbourne.
It ought not to be news to any newspaper that there is
absolutely no bank association in N.S.W. One existed up
lo the time of the trouble with the B.N.S.W. over the
Govt. loan, when it was broken up. The banks have a
joint clearing-house, to the maintenance of which each
contributes ; but there begins and ends their association.

companies in their hands that work is enormously in
arrear. As these officers are paid by results, the appoint-
ment of others would not tax the Treasury,
# * •
“Dumps,” from Queensland, writes us some
doleful generalisations on the jobs accomplished in letting
mines on tribute. After, for some years, paying his hard-
earned savings into a mine, he now learns that it has been
let on eighteen months’ tribute to the former manager
and others, who are also big shareholders. The conditions
are £4 a week to each working tributor, £5 a week to the
manager, and an equal distribution of gold won from the
mine between tributors and shareholders. Poor “Dumps”
innocently enquires whether the writer thinks he has
been “got at?”

Embedded somewhere in every mining manager’s
report is always a sentence framed to feed the hungry
hopes of the call-paying shareholder. Here are some
specimens culled at random from a lot of hanky-panky
Gympie(Q.) shows;—“The reef has not been broken down
in the North level, hut the previous breaking down
showed a good sprinkling of gold.” “ The reef continues
small, but I have seen some fair gold in the stone lately.”
"The reef in this level is small, but it carries fair gold.”
“The reef will average from two to three inches, but I
have seen a little gold in it.” “We are meeting good
breastheads with every appearance of a reef being near.”
These are the baits that catch the calls.

* * #
Dear Bulletin, —Melbourne newspapers for
some time past have systematically suppressed unfavour-
able quotations of local bank stock. Interested financial
magnates of great weight and circumference interviewed
sympathetic editors and pointed out that public confi-
dence was being shocked by the fact that the shares in
their banks were changing hands at panic prices, and
demonstrated that the prosperity of the nation was bound
up with that of the banks, don’t you know, and earnestly
observed that they only wanted a little assistance to
restore public confidence. So, instead of quoting actual
sales at low figures, the papers announce that there were
“ no buyers,” and the public assumes that nobody Would
have the shares at any price, and confidence is further off
than ever.—Yours, &c., D.

The position of Ainslie in the Mercantile Bank
base is distinctly peculiar. An acting-manager of the
Bank at the time of many of its most doubtful trans-
actions, he has been a non-aggressive liquidator since it
Went bung. The Crown neither duplicates him in the
alleged conspiracy, nor produces him as a witness against
his late colleagues; General opinion holds that Ainslie’s
present position is anomalous.

• • •
Two processes for the treatment of refractory
sulphide ores are being developed at the Broken Hill
Proprietary mine, and an extensive experimental plant is
being erected in each case. The first process, which is
being run by John Howell, general manager of the mine,
claims to dispose of the refractory zinc and sulphur in
the ore by a system of concentration, while the second
process—engineered by H. H. Schlapp, assistant general
manager—is a modification of the method of treatment
recommended by Prof. Schnabel, and consists of roasting
and leaching. It is understood that should the earth
open and swallow Schlapp’s plant bodily, so that not a
vestige remained, Howell would not abandon himself to
his grief to any great extent, while in case a quantity of
dynamite exploded in the vicinity of Howell’s plant, and
left nothing but a tangled mass of old iron and some
bricks, Schlapp would not be found afterwards throwing
stones at his grandmother. The patent of the successful
method of treatment will be worth a Very large sum.
*. * «
The shaft of the Broken Hill Extended mine
at a depth of 1250 ft. is still in country rock, which gives
not the slightest indication of the proximity of an ore
body. Whether or not the Block 10 lode underlays into
the Extended is one of the problems of Barrier mining.
On the assumption that it docs the directors of the
latter property propose to sink the shaft another 100 ft.,
and probably sinking will be continued until funds give
out, or the long-expected ore body is reached. The
interception of the Block 10 lode at a depth of over
1200 ft. would be an event of immense importance to the
Barrier in that it would prove whether or not the sul-
phides become less intractable as depth is attained. The
shaft is already far the deepest on the field, though
greater depths-considerably over 2000 ft.— have been
explored by the diamond drill on the Brisbane Blocks
property.
• * •
A recently-patented method of generating steam
by means of molten slag is undergoing trial on an exten-
sive scale at the Broken Hill Proprietary mine, and it is
expected that in future all the steam used on the mine—
equal, at present, to aboullooo horse power—will be gene-
rated by this method. Other mines possessing smelting
plants will follow suit. The principle of the invention is
exceedingly simple. A charge of molten slag is enclosed
in a steam-tight compartment, and is then played upon
by a jet of water, which is instantly converted into steam
and passes at once into the boiler. The main difficulty
which beset the inventors was how to renew the charge of
slag when its heat had been exhausted. The process will
effect an immense saving in coal, and will be valuable for
the speed with which a high pressure of steam can be
generated.

Uear Bulletin, —Re the Lady Augusta mine
and the Eidsvold. Eidsvold is certainly in an untried
district, but the drawback is not want of water, Eidsvold
itself being only three miles from the river BUrnett. The
total returns from the field for ’92 were about 14,0000 z.
The Lady Augusta mine is owned by a party of eleven
working men, who bought the mine with £lOOO worth of
machinery from the original co. on 16th Jan., 1892, since
when they have crushed 290 tons quartz for 12520 z. gold
—a little over 4oz. Sdwts. per ton, the biggest return
being soz. ISdwts. and the smallest 3oz. 4dwts. How’s
that for an average ? The shareholders say that they will
stick to the mine and work it out, which working out
should take something like six years at the present rate of
going. —Shareholder.

The writer was premature in saying, a month
ago, that sufficient water had been caught in the Spa-
Jessop dam to enable sluicing to commence, albeit the
four inches of rainfall have more than half filled the dam,
and the water is backed some miles. Let us hope no
hitch will occur to prevent active operations of the
magnitude anticipated.
* * *
Unlike every other industry, gold-mining is not
endangered by competition. It fosters most other avoca-
tions, but competes with none, not even with itself The
discovery of £20,000,000 worth of gold would not reduce
the value of that metal by a single fraction. Which can
be said of no other metal or mineral.

beyond that halfpenny now.

Hard-up unemployed miners are numerous in
N.S.W. Travelling from one field to another they manage
to find tucker along the road. If you cannot get work
you must walk to live in the country districts of Aus-
tralia. Men never starve so long as they can walk—far
enough.

business in calling down sudden death on liars, also the

whom did Richard buy the business, we wonder? And
does the sale include what he calls his diploma? “ I vill
show you my diploma,” said the professor, on Saturday,
and thereupon fie solemnly handed down for inspection a
leaf from Brisbane Government Gazette, setting forth
that he had applied for a patent for some electric
appliance or other. And when the Professor sells the
name of Albert Riohard what is his own name going
to be? *
• • *
Another patent quartz-crusher, “Muclie’s,” has
entered the field. Th" machine consists of three semi-
circular chambers or pans about 4ft. dia., boiled together
in line, each fitted with a rocker. They are placed a little
below each other so that the tailings from one go into the
other. The crushing is done by chilled-faced heavy
rollers, 1 which, with the rocking motion roll from
one side to the other of the pans. Amalgamation is
allowed in the pan itself as well as the outer ripples which
are subjected to the same backward and forward motion.
The rockers beneath the machines, as well as the action
of the roller, reduce friction, and therefore the power re-
quired for working. The scheme looks promising.

Dear Bulletin, — Be Swamp Oak. You err in
saying that all recent local crushings are from new finds.
There has been really no recent And. Late crushings
have been from Routine Plush, Highland Mary, Storm
King, Sophia, Rising Moon, Queen of Sheba, Great
