As late as September the pious J abez Balfour,
M.P., was taking the chair at meetings of his busted
societies in London, and carrying resolutions in favour of
voluntary liquidation under his own management.
Shareholders are all the same at both ends of the world.
They take at least three months to discover that the men
who have laid their property in ruins are the least desir-
able persons to have the fingering of the debris. See how
meekly the Australian Banking Co.’s shareholders elected
Abigail as liquidator ! Balfour was a most “respectable
man, with a huge reputation for “ thrift,” a prominent
teetotaller, and a much - admired patron of alleged
religion. He gave £lOOO worth of peals of bells to several
churches, and distributed 1000 free copies of his own
portrait.

“The certainty that punishment will be swift
and certain is the best known preventive of crime. It is
sometimes said that communities are injured by these
exposures; but it may rather be imagined that what
injures a community is not strictness in inquiries and
promptness in action, but is a cynical indifference to ill
conduct and to false pretences, on the false assumption
that ‘all do it.’” (The Argus on the Anglo-Australian
Bank swindle.) It sounds like some recent Bulletin
verse put into prose.

Society (London), which recently closed up with a
£3,000,000 deficiency, have been sentenced to 12 years’
gaol, and Newman, a smaller light of the institution, to
five years. They were all pious men, especially Hobbs,
who was nearly bursting with holiness, and among them
they laid many church foundation-stones in the days of
their greatness.

The popularity of Mr. W. Franks, late of
the firm of F. T. Wimble & Co., Sydney, was
attested by the many shapes assumed by the
valedictories tendered him on the eve of his depar-
ture for England. On one day a numerously-
attended picnic to Middle Harbour; next day a
picnic to the National Park, attended by a large
company; followed later in the week by private
dinners, and the formal presentation to the de-
Rarting gentleman of tokens of esteem from the
faster Printers’ Association, the Printers’ Cricket
Association, and his late employes. And, finally,
on board the Oroya, a host of Mr. Franks’friends,
presided over by Mr. J. Millett, met and pre-
sented him, over a glass of wine, with a handsome
farewell address and a purse of sovereigns where-
with to buy a token of their esteem.
Artistic connoisseurs should visit Callan’s
Gallery, 318 George-street, Sydney, where are to
be seen (and purchased) the original pictures in
“ black and white ’’—mostly in water-colours, but
some in oils—drawn for the purpose of reduction
and engraving for the “Picturesque Atlas of
Australasia.” There are about a thousand of
these valuable originals, many of which, having
served their primary purpose, are now for private
sale at a great reduction upon their actual cost.
The pictures include the work of such artists as
Smedley (of world-wide fame), Schell (who since
his return to the United States has been appointed
art-manager for Harper Bros.), and Fitler (an
artist of much foreign celebrity), besides such
familiar Australian artists as Fullwood, J. G.
and G. R. Ashton, Macleod, Mahoney, Bilton,
Cayley, Turner, Tom Roberts, Mrs. Ellis Rowan,
and others. For the production of these pictures,
the artists travelled all over Australasia, no ex-
pense having been spared to secure the most
faithful and the highest class of work. Several
celebrated artists were specially imported from
America at fascinating salaries, and the result has
been a collection of pictures of which the
Picturesque Atlas Co. may be excusably proud.
Smedley’s work has proved quite equal to any-
thing he ever did for “Picturesque America,’
“ Picturesque Canada,” or other topsawyer
illustrated publications. It is not often that real
art-bargains are so available to purchasers.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Wesfcralian cricket team area ratherpoor lot.
North, an old Oxford player, and Orr, formerly a Mel-
bourne Club player, are the only batsmen, Bishop is,
however, a first-class trundler. The rest are below medio-
fcrity - * * •
The V.R.O.’s judicious scheme of retrenchment
in racing stakes will start with the Queen’s Birthday
meetin"'. The reduction in the added money will still
leaVe The stakes bigger than they were five years ago
when the “ boom ” was at its full height.
* * *
At latest a foolhardy individual had under-
taken to dive from the dome of Westminster Aquarium
into six feet of water below. As a precaution against
accident, his ability to do the trick was to be privately
•i tested” foot by foot after Uie first fifty.
* * *
Gr. R. Sims, a recognised champion of dogs, is
now claiming a high class of intelligence for horses. But
Fillis Prof. Sample, and all the horse-trainers who talk
f re ely about their business, agree in saying that the noble
animal is a much bigger fool than he seems. Perhaps the
uncertain comings and goings of circus hte prevent a
horse from making accurate measurements of time and
cultivating the bump of locality.
# # *
How the sculler has declined in popularity !
Lambert, of Svdney, whom alone Bubear, English cham-
pion, bars in his challenge to the world, can’t raise enough
money to follow Stanbury to America,
» * *
Railing on hard boards is the cause of many
boxing fatalities. To obviate.this danger an experiment
is hein" made in Melbourne by laying over the ring
smooth cork covered with tightly-stretched canvas.
# * *
Peter Jackson’s “ successful acting” reminds us
that when, a few years ago, Larry Foley, as Charles the
Wrestler, went down before Darapier in As You Like
It ” the "allery gods, ignorant of Shakespeare, thought
the show a “schlenter,” and demanded their money back.
« * *
Why has boxing prospered in America ? Because
they will have nothing to do with a beaten man. Con-
sequently, no man caves to go down purposely without
bein" paid a fabulous sum. This also explains the
reluctance of champions to enter into a real contest. In
America, more than in any other land, success is wor-
shipped. The States is a bitter country for failure.
# * *
Bowmont, by St. Albans from Pardon, was
deservedly awarded the prize as champion blood stallion
at the N.S.W. Agricultural Show.
• * *
Mr. Bartlett’s Maid of Munster, by Mungo
Park was voted the best trolling mare. Grand mare as
the Maid undoubtedly is, she has more the appearance of
a roadster, and is certainly not the ideal stamp of a trot-
ting-bred mare.
# * *
However, no mistake was made when the blue
ribbon was attached to Red Wind, as trotting stallion,
and Mr. Frank Hill’s imported Ariel. They were both
“ made to order ” for their respective classes.
* * *
The high jump was won by Castlebar with a
leap of sft. Sin. Spondulix, who has a record of 6ft.
9jin., was in this event, but he jumped unkindly. Castle-
bar was bred at Unanderra, is by Lord Castlebar, and has
an unbroken list of wins at the high jump.
# * *
Shannon, the fine-looking, iron-grey gelding, by
Confidence, won Tattersall’s Hunter's Plate, faultlessly
taking his six jumps at first time of asking.
# * *
Reported that the English Cricket Association
refused to grant its patronage to an Australian Eleven
unless a certain name was left out. We could name him
in one try. „
* * *
What lias become of the Melbourne Golf Club?
It started some three years ago with all the prominent
men on its members’ roll—ih fact, the list read just like
the provisional directorate of a land-bank, and a playing-
ground, consisting of several paddocks and swamps
pegged out into sixteen-feet allotments, was kindly
lent” the club by Matthew Davies. Golf is a game
resembling shinty with the fun left out, and requires
several miles of space and a helot to hump the imple-
ments of amusement. It is unlikely to catch on with the
young Australian, who prefers a game in which he can
lay-out a personal enemy or sloush an umpire.
' Corbett and Mitchell are to fight at New York
for £BOOO. It will not be a hippodrome. No one on
earth would trust Mitchell.
• • •
Sam. Hordern’s Sydney Cup win with Realm is
a bit of the luck belonging to the man of judgment*—the
one with plenty of money, and the sense to buy the best-
bred horses, and retain the services of the best men. Mr.
Hordern is apparently going to run his stabl i on the
James White principle.
* * *
Messrs, “Harry” Dawson and “Tom
Slattery, M.’sP. (N.S.W.) were travelling express to
Melbourne, not long since. In the same carriage was a
Hebraic-looking gentleman, who, noticing their, flash their
Parliamentary passes to the guard, coolly pulled out a
gold coin attached to his watch-chain each time the official
came round. When they got to Albury the gentleman
from Jerusalem pulled Minister Slattery on one side, and
in a mysterious whisper enquired: “ Say, old pal, will
this schlinter work all right on the Victorian lines.
Harry Dawson says that about the first man he saw upon
Flemington course was their fellow-traveller with a gin-
case and three cards, inviting a crowd of yokels to find
the Lady Queen. # #
On a recent Sunday there entered a N.S.W.
country township this procession—two well-clad poten-
tates in a buggy, a freshly-shot turkey depending from
the hack, and a mounted policeman, who rode behind.
The natural inference was that the policeman vyas on the
trail of some sinner who had been turkey-potting on the
Sawbath. Not so. One of the potentates was a Judge,
who was to open the local Quarter Sessions next flay ; and
the policeman humbly carried the turkey into his Honor 8
hotel.
# * #
Dear Bulletin, —The term “barrack” is evi-
dently derived from the Hebrew word “barak, meaning
<l to bless.” When the football ban-acker lulls his caco-
phonous beliowings, the Amalekites of the other side
prevail.—Yours, Sneak.

Sam Matthews, proprietor of the Sydney
'California Athletic Club, savs he has witnessed 800 fights
to a finish in N.S.W. within ihe last two years.
The Euoalyute Lozenges for colds, made by
■Colemane and Sons, are unsurpassed.
Charles Lilley is keeping himself aloof
from Queensland political parties, and playing a
lone hand for a while. In a word, he won’t be
leader until he Sees a team to lead.
The ‘‘lady friend” of a well-known
cricketer, now on his voyage ’Ome, boarded the
boat at Adelaide, just as he was congratulating
himself on having left her safely behind. How
lively she will make things on the voyage for her
erstwhile devoted one and his party !
Apropos of N.S. VV. Premier Dibbs’ insol-
vency, resignation of his Parliamentary seat, and
re-election, it is contended by some that a M.P.
who “ becomes ” bankrupt remains as a “ become”
bankrupt until he obtains his certificate of dis-
charge, and that, under the 26th section of the
local Constitution Act, he cannot validly sit asa
member until he does obtain such certificate. The
recent electioneering manoeuvres of lately-bank-
rupt J. L. Dow, in the Kara Kara electorate
"IVic.), may be considered as applicable to this
argument.
“ Glimpses of the World,” a portfolio of
photographs issued in Australia by the Oceanic
Publishing Co. of Sydney, is a publication so
honestly worth the modest price demanded for it*
that it must everywhere command enthusiastic
praise. In this album, for the first time, Ihe
Bulletin sees the perfection of photography
wedded to the very apotheosis of printing. In it
are vividly illustrated the world’s masterpieces of
architecture and art, and the most celebrated
historical and other scenes of natural beauty. On
artistic and educational grounds, this unique
publication should be in every house which can
afford it.
EVERY NOTABLE EVENT
Notches itself in the calendar of time. The
origination of Wolfe’s Schnapps signalised an
epoch, and will live as long as man exists.

Ode to Austialia.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
O, princess, arise from thy slumber! look down on thy
world-girdling seas,
On hills where thy herds have no number, take arms for
the pride of all these ;
The sapphire sweep of wide water that circles thee round
with its might,
Thy days never sadder nor shorter than summer and
summer’s delight,
Thy long golden hours that mellow the fruit on thy fig-
trees and vines
Till the grass of thy pastures grows yellow as the treasure
of ore in thy mines ;
Earth’s veins that are running with riches deep down from
the sight of the sun,
The thousands that toil in thy ditches to mock, to elude,
to outrun;
Take arms for thy pastures unbounded that wait for the
husbandman’s toil,
For thy kingdom by heroes once founded who banished
the slaves of the soil;
Take arms for the land once polluted with toil that was
bitter as death,
With poisonous weeds now uprooted that leave yet a
taint of their breath ;
Who knoweth the wealth in thy fastness of mountain and
river and creek!
Oh, awful in wonder and vastness ! Shall we preach to
thee, we who are weak?
We live but a day of thine ages, whose land in its infancy
lies
With a history all blank in its pages, in the lap of the
seas and the skies ;
Could we sleep, and in centuries waken, and view thee
the youngest of lands,
The world wuth the noise of thee shaken, the fruits of the
years in thy hands,
Thy foot on the neck of Oppression, and Science thy
sceptre of sway,
When the thoughts of thee find their expression, the
fumes of thy sleep roll away,
Could we see thee then—ah, could we know thee, we, ser-
vants and children of thine,
Who pour forth our hearts’ blood to show thee, that thou
shouldst be drunk as with wine ;
Could we see the fair fruit of our labours, that others long
years hence shall see.
When with music of pipes and of tabors kings seek thee
from over the sea,
And offer thee richness of spices, and clothe thee with
purple and gold,
And the fame of thy glorious devices in the sight of all
lands is unrolled ;
Thy banner raised high in defiance, blood-red from the
patriot’s heart,
The electrical lode-star of Science, the beauty and passion
of Art!
Ah, rouse thee, thou passionless Peri! thy des iny rolls
beyond sight,
Beyond hearing of us who are weary, and sleep in thy
star-woven night—
Ah, shake off the shadows of failure, the heights for thy
climbing are steep!
Take arms for thy glory, Australia, and sow that the ages
•nay reap!
NOKOMIS.
“Bridget, where did you get that black
eye?” “Please’m, my cousin get it timme, m’m,
but what will the payple say to me having an oye
like that, and no husband?”
In a “full” House of Commons 200 of
the sitting members have to stand —on one leg or
otherwise. The members who can’t find seats
have a natural tendency to be agin’ the Govern-
ment.
Boston (U.S.A.) Globe thieves “ The
Baldheaded Metamorphosis,” originally written
by “ The Dipsomaniac ” for The Bulletin, excises
portions in order to crowd it into less space, and
publishes the mutilation under the heading,
“ Dastardly Handiwork’’—which it perhaps was.
Then Snap-Shots,” one of the English pro-
fessional pirates, professing to be honest for the
nonce, republishes the disfigurement and credits it
to Boston Globe. The Bulletin is copyright, in
Australia anyhow, and may one day suddenly give
a heap of trouble t<> the British pirate rags. By the
way, Manchester Clarion, a paper which has
managed to get quite a reputation in England, is
usually pretty well loaded with unacknowledged
Bulletin matter.
“Jock” Baird, father of the recently-
deceased “ Abington ” Baird, though a self-made
man, was very ignorant; and like most ignorant
men, fond of boasting on the slightest provoca-
tion. He once, it is related, met at a Glasgow
pub., a kindred spirit, in a once well-known cock-
fighter and horse-racer, one “Jimmy” Murray;
and they began arguing as to which of them knew
the most on some subject. “What do ye ken
aboot it,” sneered Baird. “ Why, I bet ye .£5 ye
canna say the Lord’s prayer.” “Done!” said
Murray, as he planked down the money. Then he
chanted : “The Lord’s my shepherd, I’ll not
want; He makes me down to lie; in pastures
green He leadeth me, the quiet waters by,” (23rd
Psalm). “ Aye, mon ! but I didna think
ye could say it; and ye say it weel,” said
Baird, as the money was handed over. It is said
that it was “Jock ” Baird who, being desirous of
possessing a library, called on a Glasgow publisher
to give the order, and when asked if he would
have them bound in calf or morocco, exclaimed :
“Guid God! can they no be done in Glasca ?”
When the old man was dying he sent for a clergy-
man and asked him if he thought it would do him
any good in the next world if he left something to
the church, “ Weel,” answered the canny sky-
?ilot, “ it’s worth tryin’. It could do nae harm.”
ifith the result that Jock Baird left .£250,000 to
the Kirk, which fund now bears his name. Good
old chestnuts! By the way, “Abington ” was
christened either William or Robert. He assumed
the name of “ Abington ’’when he took to horse-
racing, because, he said, it sounded better.

An American on Federation.
Dear Bulletin, — In common, I presume,
with many other Americans, 1 am frequently
asked if the United States would not rush to the
assistance of England if that small but aggressive
island were threatened with national extinction
by her long-suffering neighbours ? The manner of
putting the query invariably implies the answer
expected—an answer I am not prepared to make.
Disguise it as diplomats and politicians may,
England has no love for the United States, and
Uncle Sam is not slow to recognise this fact.
Americans are not likely to forget the attitude of
England during the great War of Secession, when
the very existence of the Republic hung in the
balance. At that time the “respectable” and
“influential ” classes in England openly expressed
their sympathy with the rebels, and put that sym-
pathy into practice whenever they could do so
with apparent secrecy and safety. Every battle-
field held by the Northern troops, every Southern
stronghold carried by them, bore silent testimony
to the treachery of England in captured muni-
tions of war : cannon cast in English foundries,
rifles from English workshops, swords and
bayonets bearing the brand of Sheffield makers, all
gave the lie to the neutrality expressed by the
English Government. The sham character of
this neutrality was exposed when it became
known that the Alabama had escaped from an
English dockyard with the direct connivance of a
.Cabinet Minister. Neither have Americans for-
gotten that Gladstone enthusiastically hailed
Jefferson Davis as the “founder of a new nation.”
When, during the struggle, Henry Ward Beecher
visited England, with the avowed object of placing
the cause of the North in its proper light before
the people of that country, press, pulpit and plat-
form combined to cover him with contumely and
abuse, while no expedient was deemed too low or
brutal, if only it promised to deprive him of a
hearing.
# m •
The present policy of the United States,
founded as it is on the political history of the
country, is entirely opposed to any interference
in foreign quarrels, on any pretence whatever,
and no Administration would be tolerated that
presumed to reverse this policy. Not only can
there be no possible sympathy between a republic
and a monarchy, no matter how “constitutional”
that monarchy may be, but the people of the
United States have long ceased to be English,
or even Anglo-American. The “aristocracy”
of New York and Philadelphia are of , Dutch
lineage, that of New Orleans claims a French
ancestry, 40 per cent, of the population of
Chicago is German, or of German origin, while
the great States of the North-west —Wisconsin,
Minnesota and the Dakotas —owe their rapid de-
velopment to the peaceful invasion of thousands
of the hardy peasantry of Germany and Scandi-
navia. The four hundred Anglomaniacs who are
supposed to constitute the elitdol American society
are of no consequence whatever; they have no
sympathy with American aspirations, and do not
exert the slightest influence on American thought.
Should they all, by a happy accident,"be sunk in
the middle of the Atlantic, while on their annual
exodus to Europe, they “never would be missed.”
* # •
The everlasting cant about the federation
of the English-speaking peoples of the world is
very tiresome, and about as sensible as would be
a proposal that the porridge eating or whisky-
drinking nations of the earth . should federate.
The people of the United Slates speak English,
or their version of English, simply because
they recognise the convenience of one uni-
form tongue for business- and other pur-
poses, and have up till now been too busy
to consider the expediency of any radical change
in the speech that has come down to them from
colonial days. That they do not regard the
English language as specially sacred is shown by
die liberties they take with its orthography and
irthoepy, while Jefferson took the earliest oppor-
tunity of substituting a rational decimal coinage
for the absurd but venerable usage of pounds,
shillings, and pence.
# * #
But such a federation, we are told, would
have power to dictate peace to the rest of the
world. Where, would be the advantage? England
does not want peace except at her own price, while
the United States would certainly not regard an
European war as an unmixed evil. The imme-
diate prospect of such a war would mean big
orders for all kinds of munitions and supplies,
with consequent increased employment and better
pay for a vast number of American artisans;
while a prolonged struggle would raise the price
of wheat and pork and thus benefit American
farmers and hog-raisers. So far as the squabbles
of England and Europe are concerned, Uncle Sam
will never be other than a spectator; an interested
spectator, ho frankly admits, if perchance any
profit may thereby accrue to him. Neither does
he court any alliances beyond the sea. He simply
hasn’t got to.
Maorilancl.

When the amorous male spider advances
towards the female, twice his size, she either
embraces or cats him—not both, like women.

A young lady has bequeathed to Camille

of Ins books bound with it as a memento.
“ Terrible the horrors that exist in the
insect world. Can we imagine the terror of a fly,
with its highly sensitive organisation, and micro-
scopic eyes, entangled in the web of a spider with
all its horrible appearance and repulsive form,
ordained to destroy it. or the sufferings of an
insect pierced by the frhvcumon, and in whose
body an egg is laid, soon to be hatched, and on
which the young will feed day by day until the
victim dies of exhaustion; what extravagant mind
has ever created an imaginary internal flame worse
than this ?”
Says Adelaide : —
Gippsland is the garden of Australia, but it is a ghastly
sight none the less, because of the land-monopoly which
has been allowed to eat the heart out of Victoria under
the regime of Munro, Davies, Beni, and their class. When
one sees a little clearing of a couple of acres somewhere
amid the vast forests of grabbed land, and sees trees three
or four years old loaded to the ground with fruit, the
traveller’wonders why the cultivator should go to Mildura
or Renmark to grow by costly irrigation what here springs
naturally from the fertile, well-watered earth. On the
borders of the northern deserts, in the hideous, mirage-
bounded plains of the Riverina, the “ cookies” are wast-
ing their energies in an effort to grow wheat, and the un-
employed are travelling the streets of the capitalSj while
in Gippsland, and, for that matter, in every province of
Australia, are thousands of the iiipst fertile acres, with a
bounteous rainfall, shut against the toiler—but surely not
for ever?—fly the fat man in the White waistcoat.

More Australian Testimony.
THE SECRETS OF SUCCESS.
Mr. J. B, Warbrick, or “Joe Warbriek,” as lie
is familiarly known, was captain of the New
Zealand Native Football Team (better known as
“The Maoris’’), which visited the Old Country
and gave such a good account of itself during the
season there of 1888-8!). This team firmly believed
that St. Jacobs Oil is one of the secrets of success
in football, and kept itself in prime condition by a
free use of it. The team was so unanimous in its
verdict that the matter is worthy of special men
tion. Mr. Warbrick, in conversation with one of
the representatives of the Ohas. A. \ ogeler Co.,
in Sydney, after their return, stated : “ I have
much pleasure in testifying to the efficacy of St.
Jacobs Oil. I have used it constantly since my
first visit to New South Wales in 1884, having
then injured my knee in our first match ; and dur-
ing our tour in Grcsxt Britain wo have used noth-
ing else, and the New Zealand Native Football
Team all speak in the highest terms of its
qualities. In fact, so much did we think of it
that we hardly ever travelled without a case
of it.’’
AN ENDORSEMENT.
Mr. J, R. Scott, the successful proprietor of the
leading hotel in Gisborne, N.Z.—the Masonic
Hotel—and the able manager of the above-men-
tioned New Zealand football team on their tour
through Great Britain, endorses the above by
letter, in which he states ; “ I have much pleasure
in testifying to the wonderful properties of St.
Jacobs Oil. While there (Great Britain) I got for
the team’s use nearly a dozen different embroca-
tions, but none seemed to do the men the same
amount of good as St. Jacobs Oil. In fact, we
never considered our baggage complete without a
case of it.”

My Dear Moorabinda—
Alice Lethbridge wore a new frock in “ Esmer-
alda ” revival at Sydney Lyceum —a pale-pink
silk concertina kink, very quaintly trimmed with
emerald-green velvet. Addie Conyers seemed to
fit the clothes of her who went before, and Leila
Roze, as Phoebus, scarcely got as much twinkle
out of those rose-pink tights as is certainly con-
cealed in them. I didn’t fancy the silver knee-
pads on the military chorus—it looked (in horse
language) as if they had all been down.
There was a crowded circle. A holiday feature
of the house was hats. One man sat with a tall
hat on; a lady appeared in a bonnet; another held
a bower-pot topping-off in her warm right hand.
How jealously a large section of humanity guards
its hat ! I once heard of a very wealthy Sydney
potentate who made his poor wife nurse his silk
hat right through a ball because he “ didn’t put
no confidence in them attendants.”
There was rather a light gallery on Saturday*
and it was a revelation to see the popular favourite
who usually goes and comes to an accompaniment
of yells getting off and on in comparative silence,
and singing only a couple of verses of a mirthful
lay which telescopes out into 14 under more genial
circumstances. Verily, there is only one Gaiety
Co. and the Gallery is its prophet.
N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s annual show,
which ran a week, was opened by Lieut.-Governor
Darley. The absence of fulsome flattery reduces
a Lieut.-Governor’s performance on.such occasions
to a mere distinct enunciation of plain and fancy
statistics, since the “prophet is in his own
country.” Mrs. Darley wore black merveilleux
trimmed with grey and black satin embroidery; a
black lace visite and a black bonnet with a crown
of spring-green silk, and carried a lovely hand-
bouquet of orchids, presented by Mr. J. T.
Wilshiro.
Other ladies on the dais (very charmingly got
up with a floral border) were Mrs. Bowden-Smith,
in shot-black and copper silk, with a _ copper
design, and trimmed with black moire, pink silk
and black lace —black bonnet with a pale-pink
feather; Miss Bowden-Smith, in yellow crepon
with black velvet bands, and wide black hat,
trimmed with rainbow ribbon; the Misses Darley,
in shades of pink ; Mrs. John See, in striped sage
green, Terry velvet; Miss Burdett-Smith, in black
cloth trimmed with jet, black hat and feathers.
Everybody cheered the Acting-Governor when
cheers were called for. A wise crowd always
cheers a judge. It sounds as if none of them had
ever been hanged, and so on. After the opening
ceremonies came the banquet, for sfentlemen only
—the Society providing an airy snack for the
ladies, who, of course, invariably prefer tea to
champagne.
I here remember a remark made by a
Sydney philosopher in a discussion over wifely
good qualities. According to him, no sensible
man ever pines for beauty and riches if
combined with a wakeful disposition. In a word,
the supreme wifely virtue, when all has been
estimated, is sleepfulness. The spouse who is
dead to the world, dreaming of new millinery with
her mouth wide open when the old man returns at
2 a.m., is the only real connubial success.
The soft music played by the Blind Asylum
band was a great improvement on the usually
brassy blast of the musician, who can actually see
his victims conversing in signs, and increases the
noise accordingly. The show all through was a
very horsey affair, and founded, as usual, on a
bedrock of hurdle and water-jumpingcompetitions.
One of the three graceful and clever equestriennes
wore a smart snuff-colored suit and rather fussy
hat to match ; the ensemble reminded one of the
dust-coloured Mounted Infantry uniform, and
looked as if it would be particularly serviceable
in a brick fielder. There were but three tandems,
despite the tandem club said to exist at Strath-
field, and only one four-in-hand—a beautifully-
appointed coach and team of bays, belonging to
Mr, S. Hordern—turned up to claim Mr. Terry s
prize. Indeed, things were rather dull vehicularly.
On opening dav, none of the club coaches did
the circuit, and, judging by numbers, sulkies are
the traps of the future. They appeared in peneot
clouds.
, Apropos lady-riders. During the judging at a
South Coast show the other day, a committeeman
remarked to “Jim”—the sole judge and an
identity of the district—“ This whip is for the
girl who wins first prize, Jim.” “But, bless my
life ” said the Mayor, who seemed to have a sneak-
ing’ idea (founded firmly on precedent) that the
best-looking girl must necessarily get the prize for
equitation, “she’s a grandmother ! Well,
retorted Jim. “ sh e ought to know how to ride by
this time, anyhow,”
At the A.J.C.’s meeting, which opened on
Easter Saturday, there was decidedly too much
black People never put forth their crisp attire
until Easter Monday, which gives the word “go”
to all the new frocks. On Saturday Mrs .W.
Mitchell sported one of the new full short
“ granny ” skirts in black silk trimmed with hoops
of black fluffy stuff and made with gigot sleeves of
green velvet—wide black hat trimmed with black
feathers and a bunch of violets pushed in at the
back Mrs. Burdekin wore the artistic gown and
hat in which she farewelled Lady Jersev-effac-
tively adding a mauve orchid } Miss Burdekin
rather effaced herself in black with coral feathers
in her hat; Mrs. Pilcher had a black silk dress
made with Russian epaulette sleeves split over green
ailk —small black hat; Miss Pilcher looked fresh
in a white Russian blouse costume; Mrs. Gordon
wore fawn trimmed with shades of green velvet;
her sister, Miss Nellie Hill, wore a military-look-
ing palet6t of invisible blue cloth over a black-
striped silk skirt —spikey little hat; Mrs, J. W.
Flood sported strawberry woollen trimmed with
black velvet; and a millionaire’s wife sat and
shivered in a thin white silk.
Sydney Cap dressing at Randwick on Easter
Monday seemed to indicate that everything is
worn but umbrella skirts, and that a great deal
more depends upon what is in the dress than the
make of the garment itself. I only saw one model
of the modified 1830 toilet, but as the wearer
hadn’t carried it out to the coal-scuttle bonnet, I
had to disqualify her as a harmonious whole. For
the benefit of the uninitiated, let me say that the
upper idea of the 1830 is obviously borrowed from
a kite, and the lower from a tar-barrel with a reef
or two let out behind, and that I don’t think it
will be tremendously popular for artistic reasons.
I didn’t see any crinoline. Mrs. Willie Mitchell
went closest to the fatal hen-coop in a voluminous
skirt that swayed suspiciously like hoops, but I
liked her long coat with triple cape of black
velvet. Mrs. Rupert Clarke looked rather after-
date in a Russian blouse turn-out of dark-blue
figured in white and trimmed with white braid,
wide black hat and feathers. A Melbourne girl
did the lawn in immense black-and-white checks.
And, apropos Melbourne folks, a visitor from over
the border said she couldn’t help remarking how
pleased and happy the crowd looked compared
with the depressed visages which have been turn-
ing out lately to make a Victorian holiday.
It is evident that violent, “ vulgar ” colours —
colours that have for a long time been left to
Chinese lanterns and cheap wall-paper—mean to
be in evidence this season. One lady of obviously
correct style was in violet cap-a-pie, as if she had
been dipped in violet ink ; another had trimmed
a purple dress with pea-green, and produced the
effect of a neutral-tinted person seen through
stained glass. The Admiral’s wife and daughter
were soberly arrayed. Miss Fosbery wore a dull
mulberry epingle with yoke and big sleeves of
black velvet—little hat to match; Miss M‘Donagh
sported seal-brown and blue epingle, trimmed
with pale-pink silk and brown velvet, beef-eater
hat, trimmed with magenta; Miss Nellie Hill
wore an attractive combination of gold and brown
checks; Miss Myra Kemble was muffled up in a
“ Home’’-made coat of black cloth, trimmed with
gold—small felt hat trimmed with velvet and
tartan. There wasn’t an enormous attendance on
the members’ stand, but an Easter Monday crowd
surged in other portions of the course.
At Her Majesty’s, on Good Friday night, Mr.
Rignold and Mr. Huenerbein got up the most
picturesquely-staged concert on record. The stage
was lit with a dim religious light, and the white-
robed chorus (why did they put all the old grand-
mothers in the front row ?) grouped against Brutus’
tent-curtains formed a very devout mise-en-sclne,
except that one choristeresse bobbed up and
down as one who bathes without wanting to get
wet, and waved a blue-and-white fire-screen to
admiring friends in front. There was an over-
flowing circle, mostly “snipped ” and unbonneted,
although it is “orthodox ” to regard Bun Day as
more sacred than Sunday, and 1 don’t suppose any
of these women would dream of going to church in
an over-ventilated bedioe and a flower in her
hair. Also, there was plenty of applause. I
merely mention the V-shaped clothes and the
clapping to show what small hold old-world eti-
quette for sacred occasions has on Sydney.
Rossini’s “Stabat Mater” was the leading
feature on the bill, and Lilian Tree made her
as first soprano. Miss Tree has emerged
from the retirement of matrimony with her fluffy
hair no longer golden, and much more dress up
above than she was won’t to average in “Martha;”
also her conversational soprano was not so evenly
pleasing as of yore, altho’ the concluding O in the
difficult “Inflammatus” rang very strong and
clear, and was redemanded by a delighted house.
The avidity with which the public come for more
shriek always makes me wonder why the steam-
piano isn’t more popular. Far away the most reve-
rent singer on the stage was “ Daddy ” Hallewell,
who sang in true oratorio style. Mr. Wa[she and
Mrs. Riley were in average form, though not
sufficiently contrite spirits for the Tribulation
music, but Signor Hazon shook his stick at the
crowded stage with highly satisfactory results.
Miscellaneous sacred items concluded the rose-
pink bill.
Mr. Burdekin spoilt the outward aspect of his
annual picnic to the Liedertafel on Ist April by
good-naturedly allowing the heterogeneous mob to
affix mouldy second-class carriages to the two
special cars which conveyed his guests to Peat’s
Ferry, which gave the special a very snuffy
aspect. I hear (it was a gentleraen-only affair)
that Mr. B. entertained the warblers in his usual
free-handed fashion, and except that a 7 a.m.
start was rather cruel, nothing occurred to make
the usual complimentary speeches sound like

A new society walk seems to have been develop-
ing during summer, which may be called the cork-
leg movement. The patient dots and carries one
in a modified kind of way, and I should say the
easiest means of getting at it would be to walk a
mile in one tight boot and a canoe. I merely
mention this latest society notion to warn sympa-
thetic people against pit-falls. An ignorant man
might imagine that the disciple of the new walk
was going tenderfooted, and suggest that she
should wear corn-plasters.
Which reminds me that one of the best-known
of Sydney’s theatrical treasurers has an infallible
specific for dodging head-colds in winter. It is
worth passing on, although I really don’t think it
will ever be violently popular. He simply puts a
couple of tablespoonfuls of rum into his boots in
preference to putting them where most people do,
and the effect is magical. The spirit dries up into
the soul—l mean sole —and warms the patient far
more effectually than by the Tosspot nu‘tlu»l
On dit that one of the latest Sydney society
engagements is off. Anybody who has had the
felicity of overhearing any of the numerous tiffs
of the happy affianced will receive the intelligence
with complacency. It isn’t a safe plan to try to
get all the rows over before marriage.
The observed of all observers at Randwick on
the first day was an obviously brand-new Johnny-
corae-lately, who strutted about the lawn in a
shooting-suit of brown tweed, with brown woollen
stockings, grey gaiters, and a sort of half-bred
Tam o’ Shanter which his solid head seemed to fill
out as you see a boiled dumpling fill its doth.
Friends at a distance will please accept this intim-
ation that he is well and happy, and treating the
little attempts at dress-occasions in the beastly
kwlinies with proper contempt bristling all over
him from the top of his head to his hobnailed
boots.
People’s ideas of what constitutes material pros-
perity differ tremendously. For instance, I over-
heard two people talking the other day, and one
said: “How’s Bill getting along?” “ Oh,
famously,” she replied ecstatically—“ splendidly !
He's bought a bicycle I "
A story comes from Adelaide about a jealous
tailor who suspected his wife so unreasonably that
she resolved to give him a lesson. As he entered
his room one evening he saw a tall and well-
dressed man looming through the gloom of the
apartment where his suspected wife was sleeping
with one eye open. With a rush the ninth part of
a man hurled the base deceiver with much force
against the wall. And then a silvery voice was
heard to say: “There you go! And our most
natural-looking dummy, too ! ”
The wisest women never go too unfledged to
social entertainments in the back country, because
the unsophisticated native isn’t broken in to it, for
one thing, and when it isn’t locally customary a
bold exposure is actually immodest, to ray way of
thinking. And sometimes women who persist in
“ peeling ” in a region guiltless of swallow-tail or
snip get rather rude experiences in return for their
sangfroid. There is a true story of one country
lady who went forth to a country concert in skirt
and shoulder-straps, and an intoxicated squatter
behind her tilted back his chair and put his two
feet on her snowy shoulders ! Of course, there
was a terrific row, and the squatter, brought to
earth with a crash, tipsily apologised ; “’Pon me
(hie) shoul, thought ’twas a (hie) pillow.” The
affair ended in the local court.
I have received an account of a “ surprise"
party ” in which the cold-blooded proceedings of
these vulgar gangs are exhibited in their full
horror. “The host,” I am told, “could not be
enticed away . . but the hostess was sent out
for about an hour ! ” Imagine the cool effrontery
of bursting into a man’s house and ordering him
and his wife out of it while you tear up their
carpets, invade their kitchen, and make hay among
their china ! The desperate man made a stand
and refused to leave his house —and I wish to
draw attention to him as a brilliant example for
others.
Slender women with understandings that aren’t
too “unfinished,” as the groom says (because a
gored skirt flopping round a pair of hop-poles is
no more alluring than a table-cloth blowing round
a clothes-prop), view the indications of crinoline
in the sartorial barometer with dumb despair, and
nobody wonders at the British anti-crinoline
league whose members take solemn oaths to come
out looking like whipping-posts rather than en-
courage bird-cages. I hope branches of the League
will flourish in Australia if the dreaded skirtal
balloon really comes to pass. An Australian
actresss, resident in Sydney, renowned for her
tasteful dressing, has given out that she will never
go on wires no matter if the whole world measures
12 yards round the hem; so, perhaps, we might
start a league straight off to discourage the dress-
makers ? It’s just as well to take the crinoline by
the wires, so to speak.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Letter received by a Maoriland resident
magistrate in re an undefended case, and handed
to plaintiff :
I am prevented by illness from attending to-day, as
ordered, and cannot send doctor’s certificate for the
sufficient reason that I am unable to pay tor a doctor or
his medicines ; nor can I send any of my family in my
place, as I have no one of sufficient age. 1 would certainly
like to pay the plaintiff had I the means, but I am afraid
that, should I be able to collect sufficient money, I should
first leave the town so as to be enabled to earn a living
for my family ; and as plaintiff's charges are such that he
can only expect one in every two customers to pay, I
have no doubt that if he finds by examining his books
that my share has been paid by some other customer, he
will return the £1 6s. I have already paid, as common
honesty would not permit him to hold it, and my need
compels me to press for prompt repayment. Kindly
suggest this to him. Being a comparative stranger here,
and it being possible that, if I am not carried off by
disease, I shall die of starvation, you might possibly be
able, from your more extensive knowledge of the town, to
direct me as to where—should I need it-1 could get a
cheap secondhand coffin, also where I could obtain the
money to pay for it.—Yours very respectfully, H. Gess
Hartb.

"Crayfish"
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A little, Witty Individual, still in a subsi-
diary ‘department i of an Australian Government
.service, and who has .occupied many positions in
both official and private life, is known as “ Cray-
fish.” He earned tfie ' • ‘ t
Late evening-, a eAßmah was hailed by a
Well-dressed, dapper man, and ordered to drive to
ft suburban address. Arrived at the destination,
cabby found that his fare had no money.
“ Well—hie ! —I shposh you’d better take thish
inshtead,” said the dapper drunk, exhibiting £i
parcel.
“What is it?
“Crayfish,» , ... „ . >■
Cabby declined', p(iW after a barney, compro-
mised .by aCpepling his fare’s “office address”—
Vn A Government department, in which it so hap-
pened there was also another party of the same
name as the dapper fare—the namesake being of
most severely respectable reputation. Cabby kept
calling for his fare, and one day was duly turned
on to—the wrong man.
“You ain’t the man I want. Ain’t there no
other ‘MisterX.’?”
“Yes.”
“ Well, I expect he’s the cove X want. At any
rate, it’s the man with the crayfish as I want ”
he had told the yarn in detail to several officials
on former occasions.
And so the sobriquet .
* # *
Crayfish w%s once a bailiff, and was sent
to put in an execution in a house, which he found
locked against him. He returned to his office and
reported his failure, at once becoming the scoff of
official comrades. After several ineffectual
attempts, a less malignant mate than the rest
told him that, if he could find an aperture of
certain dimensions in any part of the premises,
he might legally effect entrance by violence.
Crayfi-h went again forth, with gleeful dreams
of vengeance, which he sought to subdue somewhat
by potations on the road. He found a sufficient
aperture, burst into the premises, and found-
he had mistaken the house and burglarised “ next
door,” which also, unfortunately, happened to be
a house of similar description to that to which the
lost Victorian Parliamentary mace was taken !
That was a trouble which cost Crayfish all his
ingenuity to overcome.
• * #
Once, he and two others found themselves
in a seaport town, without a single cent between
them ; and they wanted to return to “ the big
city.” A plot was hatched. They were all
swimmers. Lots were drawn as to the “ parts ”
to be taken by each.
They went aboard, by sections of one, each pre-
tending not to know either of the others.
As the steamer passed out of harbour, there was
a cry of “ Mail overboard !”
Crayfish, who was seated in the saloon, rushed
frantically on deck, singing out “Where?”
plunged overboard, and saved, with difficulty, a
heavy G-ft. man. When both were safely hauled
on deck, Crayfish fell in a faint, gurgled for
“ Brandy ! ” and became the hero of the hour.
Then stepi ed forth the third conspirator, a well-
known cricketer, with, “Gentlemen!” and the
usual speech on bravery. He started by putting
p. supposed ; <£l-nofo into bis hat,, winch he then
parsed round for a collection*, Result: <£26 10s.

for his dividend. ,t
“Oh! we’fe not half done yet”—and a satis-

Ones Crawfish was Managing, clerk &
Tasrttaniah lawyer. Who. Bad some connection with
£ big concerning which had to be
taken ‘by commission ” in another province.
Crayfish was despatched there in consequence.
Having no “Gladstone” of his own, he took a
bag belonging to his boss, and, as that bag had
inscribed upon it the full name of its owner,
Crayfish became a distinguished passenger, and
very ably he personated that character.
Now, the very Judge who presided over the
evidence “ by commission ” happened to be own
brother to Crayfish’s boss ! And, what was more
curious, he had never seen his brother, who was
many years younger than he was, having been
born after the eldest son left the paternal roof-
Here was “shucks ” for Crayfish ! The Judge
embraced him fraternally, invited him to an
honorary seat on the Bench during a whole
sessions, consulted him on law-points, besought
him to be his private guest in his own house, and
treated him to a real good time, though, when
family history became the topic, Crayfish always
trembled in fear of discovery. But he got through
with his usual luck, and returned safely to his
boss with a high reputation for his successful
management of his mission !
• * •
Three months later Crayfish’s boss re-
ceived a letter from his brother the Judge, allud-
ing to the good time they had recently enjoyed,
and stating that he was about to visit the lawyer’s
city during “vacation.” The lawyer watched the
shipping-lists, and in due course saw his brother’s
name among the “arrivals.”
He called at the hotel agreed upon, and was
shown into the Judge’s room.
“ But you’re not my brother —my brother’s
quite a little man.”
The lawyer wasbig and burly.
Waiters were called, and a high old scuffle fol-
lowed until someone stopped the shindy. Explana-
tions and a brotherly dinner followed the clean-
ing away of the blood and hair and broken things.
This explains how it happens that Crayfish is
once more in a subordinate billet. But he will
assuredly rise again at the very next opportunity.

Saint Peter.
(From an old Swajmar's Point of Vic io.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Now, I think there is a likeness
’Twixt St. Peter’s life and mine,
For he did a lot of trampin’
Long ago in Palestine:
He Wad union ” when the worker!
First Began to organise,
And —I’m glad that old St. Peter
Keeps the gate of Paradise.
When the ancient agitator
And his brothers carried swags,
I’ve no doubt he very oft n
Tramped with ernpiy tucker-bags ;
And I’m glad he’s Heaven’s picket,
For I hate explainin’ things,
And he’ll think a union ticket
Just as good as Whitely King’s.
He denied the Saviour’s union,
Which was weak of him, no doubt;
But perhaps his feet was blistered
And his boots had given out.
And the bitter storm was rushin’
On the bark and on the slabs,
And a cheerful fire was blazin’,
And the hut was full of “ scabs.”
When I reach the great head-station—
Which is somewhere “off the track -
I won’t want to talk with angels
Who have never been out back ;
They might bother me with offers
Of a banjo—meanin’ well—
And a pair of wings to fly with,
When I only want a spell.
I’ll just ask for old St. Peter,
And I think, when he appears,
I will only have to tell him
That I carried swag for years.
“I’ve been on the track,” I’ll tell him,
“ An’ I done the best I could,”
And he’ll understand me better
Than the other angels would.
He won’t try to get a chorus
Out of lungs that’s worn to rags,
Or to graft the wings on shoulders
That is stiff with humpin’ swags;
But I’ll rest about the station
Where the work-bell never rings,
Till they blow the final trumpet
And the Great Judge sees to things.
Sydney.
HENRY LAWHON.

A nice-looking funeral recently pervaded
the streets of Roebourne (W. A.). An aborigine
had ‘difed in gaol, and' several "other 'aboriginal
■ briso,Uetß .were turned out to bury him. The
oerbaiied was tint in a dray and partially covered
with a bag, and his legs dangled cheerfully oyer
the back of the vehicle. Two aborigines led
the horse, and four more ran yelling behind.
About half-way to the place of burlap the pro-
cession got mixed up With a White Mail s furietfil
bf i til posing dimensions, and tvheri the 'undressed
borjise arid the barefooted howlers who accom-
panied it had got fairly entangled with the other
crowd the horse stopped short and fiatly declined
to move. Then a pandemonium arose, while the
blackfellow’s funeral yelled and cursed and
shouted and urged on the horse with violence, and
the white man’s funeral was scattered to the four
winds by the plunging of the imuriated animal,
and the deceased bounced up and down on his
cart, and his legs flew out at all sorts o* angles.
Ultimately the horse was taken out of the dray,
and the mourners ran the vehicle and the corpse
through the town at a sharp trot, and bundled
him into his sepulchre, where he now sleeps
beneath his hag in the sure and certain hope of a
blessed resurrection. Last of all the horse Was
harnessed up again, and the cortege galloped
hilariously back to the gaol amid the excited
cheers of the local small boy, and the ceremony
was over. Only the white man’s funeral has since
been making a considerable row oyer the intrusion
of the defunct black into the midst of its gtiefj
and it is rumoured that a new system is to be
adopted for the future.
When the school committee chairman
introduced to the Weyba Creek (Q.) school its new
master, Howe, he congratulated the latter upon
having “an intillijint mob of byes an’ gurnls to
insthruct.” His felicitations, however, WCte not
devoid of misgiving, for the average stay of
former masters had been stormy and short, owing
chiefly to the untameableness of hobble-
dehoys—gaunt, lanky youths who could “stick a
buck ” to perfection, and give the pedigree of every
racehorse in Australia, were great on brands, but
were dunces at “ book-learning. ” Howe wasn t
long in discovering sojne of their peculiarities.
Entering school one morning, he found
Reilly vigorously caning a smaller boy.
down that stick!” thundered the master, lor
answer. Reilly bolted, flinging away the roo of
castigation. After him pelted the schoolmaster,
and after him chivvied a couple of scholars. “Cross
the creek,” roared the hinder pals, “the cove earn t
swim!” Reilly dashed on as directed, and,
when on the bank, noticed a boat. With inspired
thought, he leaped therein, pulled out the plug,
and then dived off into the stream. Pedagogue
Howe, inwardly fuming at the escaping rebel, saw
the boat, and, in a twinkling, was afloat, poling
the craft across with a sapling. So intent was he
on watching the fugitive that he did not notice
that the boat was filling until it was in mid-stream
and the water was cooling his kneecaps. Just as
he tried to turn back, the boat went down, with a
gurgle, in ten feet of water, and the schoolmaster
roared for help, in the presence of his assembled
scholars. Reilly and another boy had to dive in
and drag the pedagogue ashore by the hair. Half-
an-hour’s hard work restored him. . . . That
was two years ago. Howe is still master of the
Weyba Creek school, and, strangely enough, has
never had any trouble with any of Ids scholars
since having been nearly drowned through the
horseplay of one of them. Moral Scare your
master to death, and then—forgive him.—S.W.S.

“The Prince of the Red Desert.”
An imaginative American journalist has pub-
lished in Frank Leslie’s'lllustrated a piece of
financial embroidery as above and purport-
ing to be “ a tale of,Australian discovery.” Some
years ago a light-coloured aborigine was shown in
our principal cities by a Mr. MThee, who brought
him from the far Nor them 'Westralia. Mr. Ladel
Barnes Gustafson, the journalist in question, made
the acquaintance of Junc/un at Melbourne Wax-
works. He found the Prince of the Red Desert
seated between two black aborigines chanting a
song which Gustafson attempts to reproduce.
Here are two verses :
THE SONG OF TUB RED DESERT’S SON.
Yah-nan-nan ! Yah-nan-nan ! Yo-erroe-ee !
O’er the shimmering sands,
By the feet and the hands
To the Perigul Tree !
Yah-nan-nan! Yafi-err-ree / Yoe-nan-nan-ah !
And hoarse is the moan—
When the irghilly's thrown—
Or the wild mooyumkarr.
Yah-nan-nan-oo ! Yah-nan-nan-ah I
Yah-erroe-noo-an-an! Yah-arra-nah!
Then commences the history of MThee’s search
for and discovery of the Prince.
But now reports thickened, and when, in all, three
hundred miles were made he came upon the tribe in a
torrid desert place, where the sand gave forth, instead of
grass, obstructing patches, most difficult to move in, of a
low, spikey scrub called spinifex. There were no huts,
no dwellings of any kind, and but one condition-
existence. The tribe fed on snakes, iguanas, and a kind of
wild rats which herd in packs between the sand furrows,
and upon the seeds of the Perigul tree, of which they are
specially fond. They drank from what appeared to be
naturally hollowed stone tanks, holding such rain as
chanced to fall, and from occasional springs. In the
hottest season they stretched themselves for the night
like lizards along the limbs of the perigul and other trees,
but generally slumbered half buried in the sand. And
now, as MThee came forward, a wonderful reward for
his faith and the hardships of his toilsome march met
him, when forth from his black people stepped the
golden-haired Jungun, he whom, though but as any one
of his tribe in their eyes, I have chosen, not unwarrant-
ably, to call—The Prince of the Red Desert! A savage,
indeed, with the habits, tjje heredity, and the ineffaceable
stamp of the wilderness upon him, but a man with a head
more commanding than rests on the shoulders of any
known savage king, or of most European potentates ; and
with brows and temples of a model to match with any in
the deliberate conclaves of the civilised world.
After this, Mr. Gustafson waxes eloquent over the
fortunes of Jungun, Dr. Bevan, Baron Mueller,
and a ‘‘clever Melbourne journalist,” unite in
forming interesting theories concerning his origin.
But the idyllic part of the affair is the conclu-
sion. Gustafson drives out to Hawthorn to visit
Mr. Lindt, the well-known photographer. There
the following episode takes place :
While I was admiring this beautiful studio, with its
wealth of interesting, unfamiliar things made by an un-
familiar race, a portiere at one end of the room was drawn
aside and a gentleman entered wearing a suit of dark-blue
serge. His bearing was unassuming and precisely what
is meant in the best use of the word aristocratic. He
carried his hat in one hand, on the little finger of which
was a heavy seal ring. His hands were finely shaped,
with flexible, slender, tapering fingers. He barely glanced
at me, then,turned his eyes to the curious things adorning
the walls, and so stood, leaning slightly on a cane. For
ease of carriage he might have been an habitue of the
European courts, except that ease so purely natural is
rarely acquired, though it may be met with in them.
It was the Prince of the Red Desert!
This is about the most sudden and startling trans-
formation of an aborigine—a myall—ever heard
of. Jungun, when exhibited in Sydney, was
simply an ordinary nigger of the low type common
to the desert country. By some strange freak he
was of a light yellow colour—such freaks are not
unknown in Africa ; but he was neither an albino
nor a half-caste. However, according to Gustaf-
son, the Southern Cross knows all about it, and on
the same authority the Southern Cross is to be
trusted. This is how he winds up :
And at last, after all researching, guessing, doubting,
and asserting, Jungun remains a secret, to us, as to him-
self. Yet as the existence of a secret, as such, involves its
being held within some intelligence, it follows that the
corporate secret called Jungun is known. Assuredly the
Southern Cross knows it, for bright over the desert must
have been that star-sown night in which so inexplicable a
life had its beginning. But the Southern Cross can be
trusted. It does not give ; it keeps ; and age by age into
the illimitable,.lightless depths of that serial hollow in the
heavens over which it keeps eternal guard, it gathers the
one true history of this strange, time-worn austral land—
a history that will never be written.
Ballade of ye Bewitching Barmaiden.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
O, youth so prone to lounge in bar
While yet the summer sun is high,
Whose loveliness naught e’er can mar,
Immaculate in “ cascade ” tie—
Beware of her whose drinks you buy,
Be cold and hard should she invite —
As spider once invited fly—
“ Ta-ta ! Will you be in to-night ?”
Nor youth alone ! Behind cigar
The father oft his oar will ply,
And give her gloves and jevvell’d star
And warmly wink the other eye—
The gay old dog can pose as sly
And shows to all his keen delight
When Hebe utters that weird cry—
“ Ta-ta ! Will you be in to-night?”
A fool through all your mash you are !
She’s wrapt in rouge, she’s dipt in dye !
You drink and jest and miss your car
And trudge it ’neath the midnight sky—
While she, “ unstuffed,” looks such a guy
That e’en the beer would sour with fright
Were she just then downstairs to sigh—
“Ta-ta ! Will you be in to-night ?”
Envoy,
Pals ! Of the syphon nymph be shy
Now when her powers are at their height;
Leave that refrain to foolish fry—
“Ta-ta! Will you be in to-night ?”
CARROWKEAL.
The red-headed man or woman was held
in particular admiration among the peoples of
Northern and Central Europe in pre-Christian
times, and Thor, the war-god of the North, was a
red-headed god by reason of his greatness. Then
the Christian teachers, in their anxiety to jump on
everything Pagan, laid their curse on the flaming
locks of the old deity and on red hair and its
owners in general. Every red-headed girl, from
the sixth century to the nineteenth, has an action
for damages against the Church, and should follow
it up to the bitter end.
A One-Sided Game.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With every breath of the autumnal breeze
showers of leaves fell from the willows on the
opposite bank into the Yarra. The bark hanging
from the gums flapped against the boles with
irritating regularity. The setting sun tinted the
water with a thousand shades of colour. His pipe
drew well, and in his brain were happy pictures.
In a few days he would be married .to his darl—
What was that noise? A subdued chuckle like
the first undecided notes of a laughing-jackass.
Again : Ha ! ha ! ha ! and nearer.
He turned on his elbow, then started to his feet,
for through the leaves of a shrub a few yards dis-
tant he had caught sight of a face. A pale, thin
face, half-concealed beneath a ragged, black beard,
and the eyes—black, aimless, mad eyes—were
watching him. In a moment he understood —it
was a lunatic escaped from the asylum, the roof of
which he could see from where he stood. He was
a little man. The best thing to do was to sneak
quietly away, and pretend he had not noticed the
face among the boughs.
Again that hideous, subdued chuckle.
He walked down the path. A crackling under-
foot. He looked behind him—the lunatic was
following, walking quickly with occasional jumps
in the air.. Faster he walked, so did the
lunatic—now gaining every stride. He ran ; the
lunatic gave a wild whoop and ran also. Along
the river path they went, seeing no one. He
thought of all the tales he had ever heard concern-
ing lunatics. But the other day he had read
about one overpowering a man, and, after biting
his ears off, finally killing him. And he was to
have been married in a week ! She would never
know, for, no doubt, after killing him, the lunatic
would throw his body into the river. How came
all those dead bodies in the Yarra!
A “whoop” from close behind lent fresh im-
petus to his flight. He was wet with perspiration.
His hat was gone, also his pipe; they had come a
mile and he was nearly done. He could go no
further.
The steps came nearer—he “could almost feel”
the lunatic’s “ hot breath.” He felt a hand touch
his shoulder.
“Tig!” said the lunatic, passing on with a
triumphant grin.

Fatality 1 A Macclesfield (Eng.) grocer
named Lynham had a vicious dog and resolved to
shoot it. He missed it with the first barrel and
the dog promptly bit him in the leg. Then he let
fly with the butt-end of the gun at its head and
missed again, but the second barrel exploded and
killed him. Nobody can escape when his time has
come. If the second barrel hadn’t exploded he
would either have mashed his toe with the butt-
end and died of lock-jaw, or he would have died
of hydrophobia—if the dog didn’t eat him up on
the spot.

Five of the death-claims recently paid by
the Mutual Insurance Co. of New York (U.S.A.)
present extraordinary features. The policies were
for 5000 dols. each, and the totals show :
Amount insured for, 25,000 dols. ; dividends added
by the M.I. Co., 27,851 dols.; total amount of
claims paid, 52,851 dols. ; total net premiums paid
by insurers, 24,422’51 dols; return over cost
(equivalent to interest on investment), 28,428‘59
dols.; dividend additions surrendered to pay pre-
miums (which may therefore be called forfeited
interest), 7079 dols. An itemising analysis shows
that in every case but one the dividend additions
exceed the net premiums paid; and that on each
of three policies the additions exceeded the original
insurance.
A Cape York Peninsula snake-perjury.
Pat Burke and Bill Gemmel were on their way to
the Batavia rush, and pitched tent one night near
the Coen. They turned in early, after a strong
supper of German-sausage, one of which mysteries
had been left on top of a swag that lay on the
floor between the impromptu bunks. About mid-
night, Gemmel was awakened by feeling some-
thing rough and cold on his face. He lay perfectly
still, in semi-paralysed horror. Presently the
thing moved off, and he saw that it was a snake
making straight for the German sausage.
Gemmel called to Burke and then crawled out of
the tent, and lay down outside till morning, when
he found that Pat had already brought in the
horses. “Nothing disturb you in the night?”
says Bill. “No,” replied Burke, “but I gets up
myself, feeling awful hungry. I couldn’t find a
match, but knows where the sausage was. So I
whips out me knife and cuts off a whack of the
sausage. By Christopher! warn’t it tough just,
an’ I’ve been sick ever since.” Gemmel went into
the tent and found —the remains of the snake and
sausage. The snake had choked itself trying to
swallow the sausage, and Pat had eaten a section
of snake and sausage combined.
Raymond Blathwayt has made such a suc-
cess, in England, as an interviewer that he is
bound to have imitators. One of these is young
Lord Mountmorres, whose father, it may be remem-
bered, was shot in Ireland some years ago. He
has taken to journalism as a profession, and shapes
very well indeed. He has tried his ’prentice
hand, as an interviewer, upon Phil May, and in
St. James’ Gazette made the genial caricaturist
give utterance to no end of absurd things which
never entered his head or came out of his mouth.
However, he was not quite so bad as the Leader
man, who drew upon his imagination entirely.
Phil is now suffering the penalty of greatness. A
story is told of this young Mountmorres. Some-
time after his father’s murder, he was sent to
school at Windsor. Queen Victoria heard of this,
and being desirous of doing something for the lad
—making him one of her pages, or something—
she sent for him to come to the castle. He, of
course, obeyed, and put in an appearance, but in
kneeling down to kiss Vic.’s hand he somehow
overbalanced himself and very nearly pulled Her
Gracious down on top of him. Her Majesty
doesn’t usually take such gaucheries quietly, but
on this occasion she saw the joke of the thing and
said to him, “ Well, I intended to make you one
of my pages, but you have much to learn before I
can do so.” He never received the appointment,
though.

“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at SYDNBY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bullet! n,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only payer which has a consistent and dit
tinctive Australian National Policy,
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Comnlete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
khan half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
Milly-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less-servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has over seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of nationa
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clabns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
